But we need to see if there is a sunny line that passes through two of these points. Points: (1,1),(1,3),(2,2),(3,1). Check pairs: (1,1)-(1,3) vertical, not sunny. (1,1)-(2,2) slope 1, sunny. (1,1)-(3,1) slope 0, not sunny. (1,3)-(2,2) slope -1, not sunny. (1,3)-(3,1) slope -1, not sunny. (2,2)-(3,1) slope -1, not sunny. So only sunny pair is (1,1)-(2,2) (slope 1). So one sunny line could cover (1,1) and (2,2). Then we have left (1,3) and (3,1). Can these be covered by another sunny line? The line through (1,3) and (3,1) has slope -1, not sunny. So no. Could we cover (1,3) and (3,1) with a sunny line that also passes through something else? The line through (1,3) and (3,1) is slope -1, so not sunny. So we cannot cover both with a sunny line. Could we cover (1,3) with a sunny line and (3,1) with another sunny line, but then we have only two sunny lines, one already used for (1,1)-(2,2). So we would need a third line? Actually we have only two sunny lines total. So we can't cover three points each on a distinct line. Could one sunny line cover three points? Not possible as we have only four points. So it's impossible to cover all four with two sunny lines if we only have one sunny pair. But maybe we could choose different sunny lines that are not necessarily passing through pairs from the set? For instance, a sunny line could pass through (1,1) and (1,3)? That's vertical, not sunny. Through (1,1) and (2,2) we already considered. Through (1,1) and (3,1) horizontal not sunny. Through (1,3) and (2,2) slope -1 not sunny. Through (1,3) and (3,1) slope -1 not sunny. Through (2,2) and (3,1) slope -1 not sunny. So no other sunny line through two of these points. But could a sunny line pass through (1,1) and some point outside the set, covering (1,1) only, and another sunny line pass through (1,3) and some point outside, covering (1,3) only, and then we need to cover (2,2) and (3,1) also? That would require at least 4 lines? Actually we have 2 sunny lines, so each can cover at most 2 points? But they could cover 1 point each if we want. But we need to cover 4 points. So each sunny line would need to cover 2 points on average. But we only have one pair that gives a sunny line. So we cannot cover 4 points with 2 sunny lines. So L1=x+y=3 fails.

What about L1=x+y=4? That covers (1,3),(2,2),(3,1). Then we need to cover (1,1),(1,2),(2,1) with 2 sunny lines. Points: (1,1),(1,2),(2,1). Check pairs: (1,1)-(1,2) vertical, not sunny; (1,1)-(2,1) horizontal, not sunny; (1,2)-(2,1) slope -1, not sunny. So no sunny pair. So each sunny line covers at most 1 point. So at most 2 points covered, leaving one uncovered. So impossible.

Thus for n=3, k=2 is impossible. So possible k are 0 and 1? But we also need to consider k=3? That would be all three lines sunny. Can we have three sunny lines covering all 6 points? Possibly. Let's try to find three sunny lines that cover all points. For instance, we could use lines: y=x (sunny) covers (1,1),(2,2); y=2x? covers (1,2) maybe? But (1,2) to (2,4) slope 2, sunny, covers (1,2) only; y=3x? covers (1,3) only; then we need to cover (2,1),(3,1) maybe with another line. But we have only three lines. Could we cover more points per line? A sunny line could pass through three points if they are collinear? Check if any three points in the set are collinear with slope not 0,∞,-1. Points: (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) not in set. (1,2),(2,3) not in set. (1,3),(2,2),(3,1) are on x+y=4 slope -1, not sunny. So no three points with a sunny slope. So a sunny line can cover at most 2 points. So with 3 sunny lines, we can cover at most 6 points, exactly the number needed. So we need a perfect covering: each point lies on exactly one of the sunny lines? Actually could be overlapping but total coverage must cover all points. Since each sunny line covers at most 2 points, to cover 6 points we need each sunny line to cover exactly 2 points, and all points distinct. So we need a partition of the 6 points into 3 pairs, each pair collinear on a sunny line. The pairs must be such that the line through them has slope not 0,∞,-1. So we need to find 3 disjoint pairs from the 6 points such that each pair's line is sunny. The points are: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(2,1),(2,2),(3,1). Let's list all sunny pairs (lines with slope not 0,∞,-1) from the set:
- (1,1)-(2,2): slope 1, sunny.
- (1,1)-(3,1): slope 0, not sunny.
- (1,1)-(1,2): vertical, not sunny.
- (1,1)-(1,3): vertical, not sunny.
- (1,1)-(2,1): horizontal, not sunny.
- (1,2)-(2,2): horizontal, not sunny.
- (1,2)-(2,1): slope -1, not sunny.
- (1,2)-(3,1): slope (1-2)/(3-1) = -1/2, sunny.
- (1,2)-(1,3): vertical, not sunny.
- (1,3)-(2,2): slope -1, not sunny.
- (1,3)-(2,1): slope (1-3)/(2-1) = -2, sunny.
- (1,3)-(3,1): slope -1, not sunny.
- (2,1)-(2,2): vertical, not sunny.
- (2,1)-(3,1): horizontal, not sunny.
- (2,2)-(3,1): slope -1, not sunny.
So sunny pairs: (1,1)-(2,2), (1,2)-(3,1), (1,3)-(2,1). These are three disjoint pairs covering all six points. So we can take lines L1: y=x (through (1,1),(2,2)), L2: through (1,2) and (3,1) equation y = -1/2 x + 2.5, L3: through (1,3) and (2,1) equation y = -2x + 5. All have slopes 1, -1/2, -2, none 0,∞,-1. So they are sunny. So k=3 is possible. So for n=3, k can be 0,1,3. Is k=2 possible? We argued no. So possible k: 0,1,3.

Now we need to generalize for n≥3. Determine all k for which there exist n distinct lines covering the points (a,b) with a,b positive integers, a+b ≤ n+1, and exactly k of them are sunny.

First, note that the points are all in the first quadrant, on or below the line x+y=n+1. We need to cover them with n lines. The total number of points is N = n(n+1)/2. Each line can cover many points, but we need to see constraints on how many points can be covered by a line that is not sunny (i.e., slope 0, undefined, or -1). Also, we need to consider the possibility of using lines that are not axis-aligned but still cover many points. Perhaps we can cover all points with 3 lines (like x=1, y=1, x+y=n+1) but then we need to cover points with a>1 and b>1 and a+b<n+1? As we saw, for n=3, x+y=4 covered (1,3),(2,2),(3,1) but not (2,1) etc? Actually for n=3, x+y=4 covers (1,3),(2,2),(3,1). Then x=1 covers (1,1),(1,2),(1,3) and y=1 covers (1,1),(2,1),(3,1). So points (2,2) covered by x+y=4, (2,1) by y=1, (1,2) by x=1, (1,1) by both, (1,3) by x=1, (3,1) by y=1. So all covered. So three lines (non-sunny) suffice. For general n, can we cover all points with three lines: x=1, y=1, and x+y=n+1? Let's check. Points with a=1 are covered by x=1. Points with b=1 are covered by y=1. Points with a>1 and b>1: Their sum a+b is at least 4. Could they be on x+y=n+1? Only if a+b = n+1. But what about points with a>1,b>1 and a+b < n+1? For example, n=5: points (2,2) sum=4, (2,3) sum=5, (3,2) sum=5, (3,3) sum=6, etc. The line x+y=6 (n+1=6) covers (2,4),(3,3),(4,2) etc. But (2,2) is not covered. So three lines are not enough in general. So we need more lines.

But we have n lines. So we need to design a configuration that uses exactly n lines (distinct) to cover all points. Possibly we can use lines that are all parallel to x+y=0? That would be slope -1. But those are non-sunny. If we use many such lines, they are all non-sunny. Could we cover all points with n lines of slope -1? For each integer t from 2 to n+1, the line x+y=t passes through points (a,b) with a+b=t, a,b≥1. That's t-1 points. So the union of lines x+y=t for t=2,...,n+1 covers all points because any point (a,b) has sum a+b = s where 2≤s≤n+1, and it lies on line x+y=s. So we can take the n lines: x+y=2, x+y=3, ..., x+y=n+1. These are n lines, all parallel to x+y=0, so all non-sunny (since slope -1). They are distinct (different intercepts). So we have a set of n lines, all non-sunny, covering all points. So k=0 is always possible. (For n≥3, we have n lines, but note: For n=3, we have lines x+y=2,3,4. That works, as we saw with x+y=2 covers (1,1) only, x+y=3 covers (1,2),(2,1), x+y=4 covers (1,3),(2,2),(3,1). So indeed all points covered. So k=0 is possible for all n≥3.)

Now, can we have k=1? Need exactly one sunny line. That means we have n-1 non-sunny lines and 1 sunny line. We need to cover all points. The non-sunny lines are those with slope 0 (horizontal), undefined (vertical), or -1 (slope -1). We can choose them arbitrarily, but they must be distinct. We need to see if we can cover all points with n-1 non-sunny lines and 1 sunny line. Is there any obstruction? Possibly the sunny line must cover some points, but the non-sunny lines could cover many. Since we have n-1 non-sunny lines, we might try to use lines that are axis-aligned: x=1 and y=1, and then lines of slope -1: x+y = t for some t. But we have n-1 such lines; they could be chosen as the lines x=1, y=1, and x+y=2,3,...,n? That would be n lines? Actually x=1, y=1, and x+y=2,...,n gives total: 1+1+(n-1)= n+1 lines, too many. We only have n-1 non-sunny lines. So we need to choose a set of n-1 non-sunny lines that together with the sunny line cover all points.

We need to find for which n (≥3) there exists a configuration with exactly 1 sunny line. Possibly for all n? Let's attempt to construct.

Idea: Use the sunny line to cover a set of points that would otherwise require many lines, and use the remaining non-sunny lines to cover the rest. Since we have many non-sunny lines, we can use lines like x=1 and y=1 (2 lines) and then lines of slope -1: x+y = t for t = 2,3,...,n? That would be n-2 lines? Let's count: x=1, y=1, and x+y=2,...,n gives: 2 + (n-1) = n+1 lines, too many. But we only need n-1 non-sunny lines. So we could drop some lines. For instance, we could use x=1, y=1, and then x+y=2,...,n-1? That gives 2 + (n-2) = n lines. But we need n-1 non-sunny lines. So we have one extra line. So we need to replace some non-sunny lines with sunny line? Actually we need exactly 1 sunny line, so the rest are non-sunny. We have n lines total. So we need to choose n-1 non-sunny lines and 1 sunny line. The non-sunny lines could be chosen as a set that covers all points except maybe some that are covered by the sunny line. So the sunny line might be used to cover some points that are not covered by the non-sunny lines.

We need to see if we can cover all points with n-1 lines of slope 0,∞,-1 and one line of another slope. Possibly we can use lines: x=1, y=1, and then lines of slope -1: x+y = t for t = 2,3,...,n-1? That's 1+1+(n-2)= n lines, all non-sunny. But we need exactly 1 sunny line, so we cannot have all non-sunny. So we need to replace some of these non-sunny lines with a sunny line. But if we replace a line, we still need to cover its points. Perhaps the sunny line could cover many points that were previously covered by multiple non-sunny lines, and then we can drop some non-sunny lines to reduce the count to n-1, and add the sunny line. So we need to find a set of n-1 non-sunny lines (maybe including x=1, y=1, and some x+y=t) and a sunny line such that their union covers all points.

Let's try to design for general n.

First, consider the set of points: (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b ≤ n+1. We can try to use the following lines:
- L0: x=1 (vertical, non-sunny). This covers all points with a=1, b from 1 to n.
- L1: y=1 (horizontal, non-sunny). This covers all points with b=1, a from 1 to n.
- For the remaining points (a,b) with a≥2, b≥2, and a+b ≤ n+1, we need to cover them with the remaining n-2 non-sunny lines (since we have n-1 non-sunny total, 2 already used) and 1 sunny line.

How many such points are there? Points with a≥2,b≥2 and a+b ≤ n+1. For a=2, b from 2 to n-1; a=3, b from 2 to n-2; ... up to a=n-1, b=2; and also a=n,b=1? No b≥2, so a from 2 to n-1? Actually a=n, b=1 not allowed because b≥2; so a from 2 to n-1. Also a=n? a=n,b=1 not; a=n-1,b=2 allowed; a=n-1,b=1 not; so the number of such points: For a=2, b=2,...,n-1 gives (n-2) points; a=3, b=2,...,n-2 gives (n-3) points; ... a=n-1, b=2 gives 1 point. So total = sum_{i=2}^{n-1} (n-i) = sum_{j=1}^{n-2} j = (n-2)(n-1)/2. Also there might be point (n,1)? No b=1 already covered by y=1. So total points = n (a=1) + n (b=1) - 1 (overlap (1,1) counted twice) + (n-2)(n-1)/2 = n + n -1 + (n-2)(n-1)/2 = 2n-1 + (n-2)(n-1)/2. But total N = n(n+1)/2 = (n^2+n)/2. Check: (n^2+n)/2 - (2n-1) = (n^2+n-4n+2)/2 = (n^2-3n+2)/2 = (n-1)(n-2)/2, matches. So indeed.

Now we have to cover these (n-2)(n-1)/2 points with n-2 non-sunny lines and 1 sunny line. That's a total of n-1 lines covering that many points. Note: The total number of points to cover is (n-1)(n-2)/2. With n-1 lines, if each line covers at most something, we need to see if it's possible.

We can try to use lines of slope -1 (non-sunny) to cover many of these points. For each t from 4 to n+1, the line x+y=t covers points with a+b=t. For a≥2,b≥2, t ranges from 4 to n+1. Then we add a sunny line L. So we have n lines: 2 axis-aligned, (n-3) slope -1 lines, and L. The union must cover all points. The points not on these n-1 non-sunny lines: Which points are missing? The union of x=1, y=1, and x+y=t for t=4,...,n covers all points with a+b ≤ n? Let's check. Points with a+b ≤ n: All such points are either on x=1 (a=1), y=1 (b=1), or on some x+y=t for t≥4 (if a,b≥2 and a+b ≤ n). For a+b = n+1, those points are not covered by these lines because the slope -1 lines go only up to t=n. So points with sum = n+1 are uncovered. That set includes points (a,b) with a+b=n+1, a,b≥1. Among these, those with a=1 or b=1: (1,n) and (n,1) are covered by x=1,y=1; the interior points with a,b≥2: (2,n-1), (3,n-2), ..., (n-1,2). That's (n-2) points? Actually number: For a+b=n+1, a≥2,b≥2: a from 2 to n-1 gives b=n+1-a from n-1 down to 2, so there are n-2 points (since a=2,...,n-1). So we have n-2 points uncovered. We need to cover these with the sunny line L. Can we have a sunny line that passes through all these n-2 points? They lie on the line x+y=n+1, slope -1, so any line through two of them has slope -1, not sunny. So L cannot be sunny and cover all of them. Could L cover some of them? Possibly L could cover a subset, but then the rest would be uncovered. We have only 1 sunny line, so it can cover at most 2 of these points (since covering 2 points with slope not -1 would require them not to be on a slope -1 line, but they are; any line through two of these points is exactly x+y=n+1, slope -1; so to have a sunny line through two of them, we would need the line to be sunny, but slope -1 is not sunny. So a sunny line can pass through at most 1 point from this set (since if it passes through two points, the line is determined and is slope -1, not sunny). So L can cover at most 1 of the n-2 points. Then the other n-3 points remain uncovered. So impossible.

Thus for n=4, k=1 seems impossible. But we need to check if there exists any other configuration with 1 sunny line. Possibly we could use a different selection of non-sunny lines, not necessarily axis-aligned and slope -1. For example, we could use vertical lines x=1, x=2, ..., x=m? But those are vertical, non-sunny. Or horizontal lines y=1, y=2, ...? Also non-sunny. But we need exactly n lines. Could we cover all points with n-1 vertical lines? For each integer a from 1 to n, the line x=a is vertical, non-sunny. But that's n lines, all vertical, covering all points because any point (a,b) lies on x=a. So we could take lines x=1, x=2, ..., x=n. That's n vertical lines, all non-sunny (since parallel to y-axis). So k=0 works. For k=1, we could try to replace one vertical line with a sunny line. For instance, take lines x=1, x=2, ..., x=n-1, and then a sunny line L. We need to cover all points. The points not on x=1,...,x=n-1: Those with a=n. But a=n points: (n,1), (n,2), ..., (n,n) but only those with a+b ≤ n+1? Actually a=n, b from 1 to 1 because a+b ≤ n+1 => b ≤ 1. So only (n,1). Also (n,0)? no. So point (n,1) is uncovered. Could L pass through (n,1) and cover it? Yes, but L must be sunny. L could be any line through (n,1) with slope not 0,∞,-1. But then we also need to cover points with a=n? Only (n,1). So that's fine. However, we also need to ensure that L does not cover any other points that we already have? That's okay; it can cover extra points, but it's not required to cover all points. So does this configuration work? We have n lines: x=1, x=2, ..., x=n-1 (n-1 lines, all vertical, non-sunny), and one sunny line L that passes through (n,1). We need to check that every point (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b≤n+1 is on at least one of these lines. For a=1,...,n-1, the point is on the vertical line x=a. For a=n, the only point is (n,1) (since b must be 1). So we need L to cover (n,1). So we can choose L to be any line through (n,1) that is sunny. For instance, L: y = x - (n-1). For n=4, L: y = x - 3. At x=4, y=1. Slope 1, sunny. Does it cover any other points? It might cover (3,0) not in set, (2,-1) etc. So fine. So this gives a configuration with exactly 1 sunny line for n=4? Let's test all points for n=4: Points: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4); (2,1),(2,2),(2,3); (3,1),(3,2); (4,1). The vertical lines: x=1 covers (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4); x=2 covers (2,1),(2,2),(2,3); x=3 covers (3,1),(3,2); x=4 is not used (we only have x=1,2,3). So points (4,1) is uncovered. L: y = x-3 passes through (4,1). So (4,1) is on L. So all points covered. The lines: x=1, x=2, x=3 are vertical (non-sunny), L is sunny (slope 1). So exactly 1 sunny line. So k=1 is possible for n=4. Wait, but earlier we thought maybe not. So our earlier attempt with axis-aligned and slope -1 failed, but this vertical line configuration works. So k=1 seems possible for n=4. Let's verify: n=4, we have 4 lines: x=1, x=2, x=3, and y=x-3. Are they distinct? Yes. Check (1,2): on x=1, (2,3): on x=2, (3,2): on x=3, (4,1): on L. All covered. So k=1 works.

Now for n=3, we had a configuration with x=1, y=1, and y=x. That's 1 sunny, 2 non-sunny (vertical and horizontal). For n=4, we have x=1, x=2, x=3, and y=x-3. That's 1 sunny, 3 non-sunny. So k=1 works for n=3,4. What about n=5? Can we do vertical lines x=1,...,n-1 and a sunny line through (n,1)? For n=5, points: a=1..4 covered by vertical lines, a=5: only (5,1) because sum ≤6 => b≤1. So we need a sunny line through (5,1). Choose L: y = x - 4 (slope 1). That works. So k=1 is possible for all n≥3? But we must check that the vertical lines x=1,...,x=n-1 are all distinct and non-sunny (vertical). And the sunny line L passes through (n,1) and has slope 1, which is sunny. So yes, for any n≥3, we can take the n-1 vertical lines x=1,2,...,n-1, and the line y = x - (n-1). That gives n distinct lines, exactly one sunny line (the diagonal), covering all points: For a=1..n-1, points on x=a are covered; for a=n, the only point is (n,1) and it lies on L because 1 = n - (n-1). So all points covered. So k=1 is always possible.

Now what about k=2? For n=3, we found k=2 impossible. For n=4, can we construct a configuration with exactly 2 sunny lines? Let's attempt to find. We have 4 lines total, 2 sunny, 2 non-sunny. We need to cover all points (10 points). Possibly we can use vertical lines x=1 and x=2, and two sunny lines that cover the remaining points? Points not on x=1 or x=2: Those with a=3 or a=4? Actually a=3: points (3,1),(3,2); a=4: (4,1). Also (1,3),(1,4) on x=1; (2,1),(2,2),(2,3) on x=2; so uncovered: (3,1),(3,2),(4,1). That's 3 points. We have 2 sunny lines. Can two sunny lines cover these 3 points? They could cover at most 2 points each, but they could overlap. However, the line through (3,1) and (3,2) is vertical, not sunny. Through (3,1) and (4,1) horizontal, not sunny. Through (3,2) and (4,1) slope -1, not sunny. So any line through two of these points is non-sunny. So each sunny line can cover at most 1 of these points. So we need at least 3 sunny lines to cover these 3 points. So this vertical line configuration fails. Could we use a different set of non-sunny lines? Perhaps we can use two horizontal lines? For instance, y=1 and y=2, and then two sunny lines covering the rest? Points not on y=1 or y=2: Those with b=3,4? Actually b=3: points (1,3),(2,3); b=4: (1,4). Also (3,1) and (4,1) are on y=1, covered. So uncovered: (1,3),(2,3),(1,4). That's 3 points, similar problem. Could we use a combination of axis-aligned and slope -1 lines? For k=2, we want exactly 2 sunny lines. The other 2 lines are non-sunny. Could we cover all points with 2 non-sunny lines and 2 sunny lines? Possibly we can use the lines x=1 and y=1 (2 non-sunny) and then two sunny lines that cover the interior points? For n=4, interior points (a≥2,b≥2) are (2,2),(2,3),(3,2). That's 3 points. Could we cover them with 2 sunny lines? As before, any line through two of these points is non-sunny. So each sunny line can cover at most 1 point. So we need at least 3 sunny lines. So impossible.

What if we use one non-sunny line that covers many points, like x=1 (vertical) covering 4 points, and then the other non-sunny line could be a slope -1 line covering many points, and then two sunny lines covering the rest? For n=4, points: x=1 covers (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4). Let's consider non-sunny line L1: x=1 (vertical). L2: x+y=4 (slope -1) covers (1,3),(2,2),(3,1). Then the union covers: x=1 points all; x+y=4 gives (2,2),(3,1) plus (1,3) already. So points covered so far: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(2,2),(3,1). Points not covered: (2,1),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). That's 4 points. We have two sunny lines. Can two sunny lines cover these 4 points? Possibly if each sunny line covers 2 points, but we need to see if there exist sunny lines through pairs among these points. Points: (2,1),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). Check pairs: (2,1)-(2,3) vertical, not sunny. (2,1)-(3,2) slope (2-1)/(3-2)=1, sunny! So line y=x-1? Actually through (2,1) and (3,2) has slope 1, equation y=x-1. That line also passes through (4,3) not in set, and (1,0). So it covers (2,1) and (3,2). (2,3)-(4,1): slope (1-3)/(4-2) = -2/2=-1, not sunny. (2,3)-(3,2): slope -1, not sunny. Then we have left (2,3) and (4,1). Can we cover them with another sunny line? The line through (2,3) and (4,1) has slope -1, not sunny. So no. Could we instead have a sunny line covering (2,3) and (4,1) if we choose a different sunny line? The only line through (2,3) and (4,1) is x+y=5, slope -1, not sunny. So cannot. So we need at least 3 sunny lines to cover these 4 points. So that fails.

What if we use non-sunny lines: x=1 and y=1? Already considered, need 3 sunny. So k=2 seems impossible for n=4. But we need to test n=5 maybe k=2 works? Let's try to find a general pattern.

The problem asks: Determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines satisfying the conditions. So for each n≥3, we need the set of possible k. Thus it seems k=2 is not possible for n=4. What about n=5? Let's attempt to construct for n=5 with 2 sunny lines. We have 5 lines total, 2 sunny, 3 non-sunny. The points: a,b≥1, a+b≤6. Thus it seems for n=5, k=2 also impossible. Possibly k=2 is impossible for all n≥3. But we need to verify for n=6 maybe? Let's try to find a pattern.

We have a construction for k=0 and k=1 always. For k=n? That would be all lines sunny. Is it possible to have all n lines sunny? For n=3, we found k=3 possible. For n=4, can we have 4 sunny lines covering all 10 points? Each sunny line can cover at most 2 points? Actually, could a sunny line cover more than 2 points? In the set of lattice points with a+b≤n+1, a sunny line (slope not 0,∞,-1) could potentially pass through many lattice points if the slope is rational, but the points are constrained. For example, the line y = 2x passes through (1,2),(2,4) etc. But (2,4) sum=6 for n=4? Actually n=4, max sum=5, so (2,4) sum=6 >5, not in set. So only (1,2) maybe. So at most 2 points? Let's test for n=4, could a line with slope 1/2 pass through three points? Points: (1,1) to (2,1.5) not integer; (2,1) to (3,1.5) not integer. So likely only 2 points. In general, a line with slope p/q (reduced) can pass through integer points with x increasing by q, y increasing by p. For points (a,b) with a,b positive and a+b ≤ n+1, the x-coordinate can increase by q, but the sum a+b would increase by q+p. Since a+b ≤ n+1, the number of such points on the line is at most floor((n+1)/(q+p))? Actually, if the line passes through integer points, the points are of the form (x0 + kq, y0 + kp). The sum (x+y) = (x0+y0) + k(p+q). For this to be ≤ n+1, k can be at most floor((n+1 - (x0+y0))/(p+q)). So the maximum number of lattice points on the line within the triangle is limited. Could it be more than 2? Possibly if p+q is small. For example, slope 1 (p=q=1) gives p+q=2. Then if the line passes through (1,1), then points (2,2),(3,3),... but (3,3) sum=6 for n=5? For n=5, max sum=6, so (3,3) sum=6, (4,4) sum=8>6, so only (1,1) and (2,2) maybe? Actually (3,3) sum=6, allowed if n+1=6, yes. So for n=5, line y=x passes through (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) (since sum ≤6). That's 3 points. So a sunny line can cover more than 2 points if the slope is 1 and n is large enough. So for n=5, y=x (sunny) covers (1,1),(2,2),(3,3). Also (4,4) sum=8>6, so only 3 points. So a sunny line can cover up to floor((n+1)/2) points? Actually for line y=x, points (a,a) with 2a ≤ n+1 => a ≤ floor((n+1)/2). So number of points = floor((n+1)/2). For n=5, that's 3. So a sunny line can cover many points. So it's plausible to have all lines sunny and cover all points. For n=4, y=x covers (1,1),(2,2). (3,3) sum=6>5, so only 2 points. For n=3, each sunny line can cover at most? y=x covers (1,1),(2,2) -> 2 points; y=2x? covers (1,2) only; y=3x? covers (1,3) only; so maximum points per sunny line is 2 (since n+1=4, a≤2). We need 5 sunny lines covering all 15 points. Each sunny line is not parallel to axes or slope -1. Could we use lines that are not just through origin? Let's try to see if k=2 is ever possible. For n=3, we argued no. For n=4, we argued no. For n=5, we argued no. So perhaps k=2 is never possible. But we need to be systematic.

We need to find all possible k for a given n. The construction for k=0 and k=1 always works. For k=n? We need to check if there exists a set of n sunny lines covering all points. Since we have n lines, all sunny. The points are N = n(n+1)/2. Each sunny line can cover at most some number of points. For a sunny line, what is the maximum number of lattice points from the triangle it can contain? The line has slope not 0,∞,-1. For a line with slope p/q in reduced form, the integer points are spaced by (q,p). The sum x+y increases by q+p along the line. So if the line passes through a point (x0,y0), then the points are (x0+kq, y0+kp). The sum is (x0+y0)+k(q+p). For these to be ≤ n+1, we need k ≤ (n+1 - (x0+y0))/(q+p). The number of points on the line within the triangle is at most floor((n+1)/(q+p))? Actually, if we start from a point with small sum, we can have many points. For slope 1 (p=q=1), then q+p=2, so we can have points with sums 2,4,6,... up to n+1 (if n+1 even) or n (if n+1 odd). So number of points on y=x is floor((n+1)/2). For slope 2 (p=2,q=1), q+p=3, so we can have points with sums 3,6,9,... up to n+1, so number = floor((n+1)/3)? Actually, if we start at (1,2) sum=3, then next (2,4) sum=6, then (3,6) sum=9, etc. So number = floor((n+1)/3)? But (n+1)/3 may not be integer, but the points are on the line, and we need to ensure that the x,y are positive. For slope 2, the points are (a,2a) for a≥1. The sum = 3a ≤ n+1 => a ≤ floor((n+1)/3). So number = floor((n+1)/3). Similarly, for slope 3, number = floor((n+1)/4). For slope 1/2, points (2a,a) sum=3a ≤ n+1 => a ≤ floor((n+1)/3) gives number = floor((n+1)/3) as well. So the maximum number of points on a sunny line within the triangle is floor((n+1)/2) for slope 1. For slope 1, it's the largest possible because 2 is the smallest sum increase? Actually, slope 1 gives q+p=2, so number of points can be as large as floor((n+1)/2). For slope 2, q+p=3, so number = floor((n+1)/3) which is less than floor((n+1)/2) for n≥3. So the maximum points per sunny line is M = floor((n+1)/2). For n odd, n+1 even, so M = (n+1)/2. For n even, n+1 odd, so M = n/2.

Now, if we have all n lines sunny, each can cover at most M points. The total points needed is N = n(n+1)/2. So the maximum total coverage if all lines are sunny is n * M. For this to be at least N, we need n * M ≥ n(n+1)/2 => M ≥ (n+1)/2. For n odd, M = (n+1)/2, so equality holds: n * (n+1)/2 = N. For n even, M = n/2, so n * n/2 = n^2/2, but N = n(n+1)/2 = (n^2+n)/2, which is greater than n^2/2 for n≥1. So for even n, n * M < N. Therefore, it's impossible to have all n lines sunny when n is even because even if each sunny line covers the maximum possible points (M), the total coverage is less than the number of points needed. However, could we have some sunny lines covering more than M? M is the maximum possible for any sunny line within the triangle. So no. So for even n, k=n is impossible. For odd n, it's possible in principle if we can achieve the maximum coverage with each sunny line covering exactly M points and all points disjoint. That would require a partition of the triangle points into n lines each covering M points, with each line having slope 1? Because only slope 1 can achieve M points (since q+p=2). So we need to cover all points with n lines of slope 1? But slope 1 lines are of the form y=x+c. For a given c, the line y=x+c passes through points (a,a+c) with a≥1, a+c≥1, and a+a+c=2a+c ≤ n+1. So the number of such points is floor((n+1-c)/2) (if c≥1? Actually for c negative, then a≥1-c? We'll consider). To have exactly M = (n+1)/2 points, we need c=0? Because if c=0, points (a,a) with 2a ≤ n+1 gives a ≤ (n+1)/2, so number = (n+1)/2. If c>0, then the sum is 2a+c ≤ n+1 => a ≤ (n+1-c)/2, so number = floor((n+1-c)/2) which is less than (n+1)/2. If c<0, say c=-d (d>0), then line y=x-d passes through points (a,a-d) with a-d≥1 => a≥d+1, and 2a-d ≤ n+1 => a ≤ (n+1+d)/2. So the number of points is floor((n+1+d)/2) - d? Actually, a from d+1 to floor((n+1+d)/2). So count = floor((n+1+d)/2) - d. For d=1, count = floor((n+2)/2) -1. For n odd, n=2m-1, then n+2=2m+1, floor((2m+1)/2)=m, so count = m-1 = (n+1)/2 -1, less. So only c=0 gives M points. So the only sunny line that can cover M points is y=x. So if we have all n lines sunny, we need to cover all points with n lines, each covering at most M points, and the total is n*M = N. Therefore, each sunny line must cover exactly M points, and the lines must be disjoint in terms of covering points (each point on exactly one line). But only one line (y=x) can cover M points. Check all possible sunny lines through these points. Consider the line through (n-2,1) and (n-1,2): slope = (2-1)/(n-1-(n-2)) = 1/1 =1, sunny. That's one pair. Then line through (n-2,2) and (n,1): slope = (1-2)/(n-(n-2)) = -1/2, sunny. Then line through (n-2,3) and (n-1,1): slope = (1-3)/(n-1-(n-2)) = -2/1 = -2, sunny. So for any n≥3, this works. Therefore k=3 is always possible.

Therefore, it's plausible that k=2 is never possible. But we must be thorough: Is there any n for which k=2 is possible? Let's attempt a general argument.

We have n lines, exactly k sunny. The other n-k lines are non-sunny (parallel to axes or x+y=0). We need to cover all points (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b≤n+1.

Consider the points on the line x=1 (vertical axis). There are n points (1,1) to (1,n). Similarly, points on y=1: (1,1) to (n,1). Also points on x+y=n+1: (1,n), (2,n-1), ..., (n,1). These are n points. The set of all points is N = n(n+1)/2.

We need to cover them with n lines. Let's analyze constraints on the number of non-sunny lines. A non-sunny line is either horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. The points on these lines are limited. For instance, a horizontal line y=c (c≥1) covers points with b=c, a from 1 to n+1-c. So it covers n+1-c points. A vertical line x=c covers points with a=c, b from 1 to n+1-c. So it covers n+1-c points. A slope -1 line x+y=t (t≥2) covers points with sum t, a from 1 to t-1, b=t-a, so it covers t-1 points.

Now, note that the total number of points is N = n(n+1)/2. The sum of the numbers of points on each line is at most the sum of the points covered by the lines, but points can be covered by multiple lines; we need the union to cover all points. So we need to select lines such that their union is the whole triangle.

We want to determine possible k (number of sunny lines). We have constructions for k=0,1,3 always. For k=n, we have n=3 possible, but n≥4 impossible. Could k=2 be possible? Let's try to prove it's impossible for all n≥3.

Assume k=2, so we have 2 sunny lines and n-2 non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines can be of three families. Let's denote the set of points not on the sunny lines. Since the sunny lines cover some points, we need the non-sunny lines to cover the rest. But we need to cover all points, so the union of the non-sunny lines must cover the points not covered by the sunny lines. However, the sunny lines can also cover some points that are also on non-sunny lines; that's okay.

We can try to use a counting argument: For a given configuration, consider the points on the line x=1. They are n points. These points must be covered by some lines. The sunny lines are not vertical, so they cannot be x=1; they could pass through some (1,b). Since sunny lines have slopes not 0,∞,-1, they could pass through at most one point on x=1? Actually, a line through (1,b) with slope s can intersect x=1 at only that point. So each sunny line can cover at most one point on x=1 (if it passes through that point). Similarly, a horizontal line y=c can cover at most one point on x=1 (if c equals b). A vertical line x=1 covers all n points on x=1. A slope -1 line x+y=t can cover at most one point on x=1 (if t=1+b, then it passes through (1,b)). So the only line that can cover more than one point on x=1 is the vertical line x=1 itself. So if we want to cover all n points on x=1, we must either include x=1 as a line, or have many sunny lines each covering one point on x=1, but we only have 2 sunny lines, so they can cover at most 2 points on x=1. The other points on x=1 would then need to be covered by non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines can be horizontal or slope -1, but they can cover at most 1 point on x=1 each (since they are not vertical). So to cover n points on x=1, we need at least n-2 non-sunny lines that cover points on x=1, but we only have n-2 non-sunny lines total. So we need to use x=1 as one of the non-sunny lines to cover all n points. Therefore, x=1 must be among the n-2 non-sunny lines. Similarly, we can argue for y=1: the points on y=1 are n points, and the only line that can cover more than one point on y=1 is y=1 itself (horizontal). So y=1 must also be among the non-sunny lines. However, we only have n-2 non-sunny lines, and we need both x=1 and y=1 to be non-sunny lines. That's fine; they can be two of them. But then we also have other non-sunny lines. So the non-sunny lines include at least x=1 and y=1. That uses 2 lines. Then we have n-4 other non-sunny lines (since total non-sunny = n-2). But we also have 2 sunny lines. So total lines = 2 (sunny) + 2 (axis) + (n-4) others = n. Good.

Now, consider the points on the line x+y=n+1. There are n points (1,n),(2,n-1),...,(n,1). These points are all in the triangle. They must be covered by lines. The sunny lines cannot be slope -1, so they could pass through some of these points. The only non-sunny lines that can cover many points on this line are slope -1 lines themselves, but the line x+y=n+1 is slope -1, so if we include it as a line, it covers all n points. So we need to either include x+y=n+1 as a non-sunny line, or cover its points with other lines. But we already have x=1 and y=1 as non-sunny lines, which cover (1,n) and (n,1) respectively (since (1,n) is on x=1, (n,1) on y=1). The other points on x+y=n+1 are (2,n-1),...,(n-1,2). These are n-2 points. They could be covered by sunny lines or by other non-sunny lines. However, a sunny line can cover at most 1 point on this line? Actually, a sunny line has slope not -1, so it can intersect the line x+y=n+1 at most once (since two lines with different slopes intersect at most once). So each sunny line can cover at most 1 point on x+y=n+1. With 2 sunny lines, they can cover at most 2 points. The other n-4 points would then need to be covered by non-sunny lines. Could they be covered by vertical or horizontal lines? For instance, the point (2,n-1) has a=2, b=n-1. Could it be on a vertical line x=2? Yes, if we include x=2 as a non-sunny line. That would cover (2,n-1) (and also other points). Similarly, horizontal line y=n-1 would cover (2,n-1) (if we include y=n-1). But we only have n-4 non-sunny lines besides x=1 and y=1. Could we include all vertical lines x=2,...,n-2? That would be n-3 lines, too many. So we need to be clever.

Alternatively, we might choose to include the line x+y=n+1 as one of the non-sunny lines. Then it covers all n points. That uses one line. So we have non-sunny lines: x=1, y=1, and x+y=n+1. That's 3 lines. Then we have n-5 other non-sunny lines? Actually total non-sunny = n-2. So we have 3 used, leaving n-5 non-sunny lines to choose. Also we have 2 sunny lines. So total lines = 2+3+(n-5)=n. Good.

Now, consider the points not on these three lines. Which points are not covered? The union of x=1, y=1, and x+y=n+1 covers: all points with a=1 (n points), all points with b=1 (n points), and all points with sum=n+1 (n points). But there is overlap: (1,1) is on both x=1 and y=1; (1,n) on x=1 and sum=n+1; (n,1) on y=1 and sum=n+1. So total covered points = n + n + n - 3 = 3n - 3? Actually, x=1 covers n points; y=1 covers n points, but (1,1) counted twice; x+y=n+1 covers n points, but (1,n) and (n,1) already counted in x=1 and y=1 respectively, so overlaps: (1,1) with x=1,y=1; (1,n) with x=1; (n,1) with y=1; and also (1,1) is not on sum=n+1? (1,1) sum=2, not; (1,n) sum=n+1, (n,1) sum=n+1; so total distinct points = n + n + n - (1 for (1,1) double counted) - (1 for (1,n) double counted) - (1 for (n,1) double counted) = 3n - 3. That's the number of points covered by these three lines. The total number of points is N = n(n+1)/2. So the uncovered points are N - (3n-3). For n≥3, N = (n^2+n)/2, so uncovered = (n^2+n)/2 - 3n + 3 = (n^2+n-6n+6)/2 = (n^2-5n+6)/2 = (n-2)(n-3)/2. That's the number of points with a≥2, b≥2, and a+b≤n+1? Actually, points with a≥2,b≥2, and a+b≤n+1 are exactly those not on x=1 or y=1 or sum=n+1? But sum=n+1 includes some with a,b≥2? For a,b≥2, a+b≤n+1, the points on sum=n+1 are those with a+b=n+1, which includes points like (2,n-1) etc. So uncovered points are those with a,b≥2, a+b≤n+1, but not on sum=n+1? That would be a+b≤n, a,b≥2. Their count is sum_{a=2}^{n-2} (n+1-a -1) = sum_{a=2}^{n-2} (n-a) = sum_{j=2}^{n-2} j = (n-2)(n-1)/2 - 1? Actually, let's compute properly: Points with a,b≥2, a+b≤n+1, and not on sum=n+1: those with sum ≤ n. So number = sum_{s=4}^{n} (s-3) = (n-2)(n-1)/2? Wait, for s=4 to n, count = sum_{k=4}^{n} (k-3) = sum_{j=1}^{n-3} j = (n-3)(n-2)/2. That's (n-2)(n-3)/2, which matches uncovered. So uncovered points are those with a,b≥2, a+b≤n (i.e., sum ≤ n). That's a triangle of points with a,b≥2, a+b≤n. Their number is (n-2)(n-3)/2.

Now, we have 2 sunny lines and n-5 other non-sunny lines to cover these uncovered points. The non-sunny lines could be chosen as vertical or horizontal lines or slope -1 lines. But note: we already used x=1, y=1, and x+y=n+1. Could we use other slope -1 lines? For example, x+y=t for t from 2 to n? But those are non-sunny. However, the uncovered points have sum ≤ n, so lines of sum t (t≤n) could cover them. Could we choose the remaining non-sunny lines to be some of these slope -1 lines to cover many points? But we have only n-5 such lines, and the uncovered points count is (n-2)(n-3)/2. For n large, (n-2)(n-3)/2 is about n^2/2, which is much larger than n-5. So we cannot cover all uncovered points with n-5 non-sunny lines unless each covers many points. But each non-sunny line covers at most n-1 points (if slope -1, t-1; if vertical, n+1-t; if horizontal, n+1-t). So with n-5 lines, max total points covered is (n-5)*(n-1) maybe, but we need to cover (n-2)(n-3)/2 points. For n≥6, (n-2)(n-3)/2 is about n^2/2, while (n-5)(n-1) is about n^2, so possible. However, the lines must be distinct and we can choose them to cover the uncovered set efficiently. The uncovered set is the triangle with a,b≥2, a+b≤n. Can we cover all these points with, say, n-5 vertical lines? For n=6, uncovered points: a,b≥2, a+b≤6. That's points: (2,2),(2,3),(2,4); (3,2),(3,3); (4,2) — total 6 points. n-5=1 vertical line x=2 covers (2,2),(2,3),(2,4) — 3 points, leaving (3,2),(3,3),(4,2) which need to be covered by 2 sunny lines? But we have only 2 sunny lines. Could we cover those 3 points with 2 sunny lines? Each sunny line can cover at most 2 points? For n=6, M=3, so sunny line can cover up to 3 points. But can we find a sunny line that covers 3 of these points? Points: (3,2),(3,3),(4,2). Are any three collinear? (3,2)-(3,3) vertical, not sunny; (3,2)-(4,2) horizontal; (3,3)-(4,2) slope -1; so no. So max 2 points per sunny line, total 4 points, but we need 3 points, that's fine if each covers 2 points? Actually 2 sunny lines can cover at most 4 points, but we have only 3 points, so it's possible if we can cover them with 2 sunny lines (each covering 2 or 1). However, we need to cover them with sunny lines; we can choose a sunny line through (3,2) and (4,2) horizontal? Not sunny. Through (3,2) and (3,3) vertical, not sunny. Through (3,3) and (4,2) slope -1, not sunny. So no sunny pair. So each sunny line covers at most 1 point from that set. So 2 sunny lines cover at most 2 points, leaving 1 uncovered. So fail.

For n=7, uncovered points: a,b≥2, a+b≤7. That's points: (2,2)-(2,5); (3,2)-(3,4); (4,2)-(4,3); (5,2) — count = 3+2+1+1? Thus it seems that with k=2, we always end up with at least 3 uncovered points that form a set with no sunny pair (they are either collinear on a line of slope 0,∞,-1). So 2 sunny lines cannot cover them. This suggests k=2 is impossible.

But we need to prove it in general. Let's attempt a general argument.

We have n lines, exactly k sunny. We want to show k cannot be 2. More generally, we need to find all possible k.

Thus for n=9, k=2 also fails.

Now, is there any n for which k=2 could work? Possibly if the uncovered set after non-sunny lines is a set of points that can be covered by 2 sunny lines. For k=2, we have 2 sunny lines. The maximum number of points they can cover is 2M, but they could also cover points that are already covered by non-sunny lines, but we need to cover all points. The crucial part is that the points not covered by the non-sunny lines (which must be covered by sunny lines) must be such that there exist 2 sunny lines that together contain them. Since sunny lines have slopes not 0,∞,-1, they can intersect the triangle in certain patterns. The uncovered set after non-sunny lines is the complement of the union of those lines in the triangle. The non-sunny lines are limited: they can be horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. If we include x=1 and y=1, the uncovered set is the triangle with a,b≥2, a+b≤n+1. That's a right triangle of points. The number of such points is (n-1)(n-2)/2? Actually, points with a,b≥2, a+b≤n+1: a from 2 to n-1, b from 2 to n+1-a, count = sum_{a=2}^{n-1} (n+1-a -1) = sum_{a=2}^{n-1} (n-a) = sum_{j=1}^{n-2} j = (n-2)(n-1)/2. For n=3, that's 1 point; n=4, 3 points; n=5, 6 points; n=6, 10 points; n=7, 15 points; n=8, 21 points; n=9, 28 points; etc. So uncovered set U has size (n-2)(n-1)/2. For n=4, U = {(2,2),(2,3),(3,2)}? Actually, a,b≥2, a+b≤5: (2,2),(2,3),(3,2) — 3 points. For n=5, U = {(2,2),(2,3),(2,4),(3,2),(3,3),(4,2)} — 6 points. For n=6, U = 10 points, etc. Now, we have 2 sunny lines to cover these U points. Each sunny line can cover at most M = floor((n+1)/2) points from U. So total max points covered = 2M. For n=5, M=3, 2M=6, which equals |U|=6, so possible in principle if each sunny line covers 3 points and they are disjoint. But does there exist a sunny line that contains 3 points of U? Actually (2,4)-(3,3): slope -1; (3,3)-(4,2): slope -1; so collinear on x+y=6 slope -1, not sunny. So no triple with sunny slope. Could there be a line with 2 points? Yes, many. So with 2 lines, max coverage = 2*2=4 (since each can cover at most 2 points from U because no sunny triple). So they can cover at most 4 points, but we have 6 points. So k=2 impossible for n=5.

For n=6, U size = 10, M=3, 2M=6, so max coverage from 2 sunny lines is at most 6, insufficient for 10 points. So k=2 impossible for n≥6 as well? Actually, for n=6, 2M=6, but |U|=10, so even if each sunny line covered 3 points, total 6 <10, so impossible. For n=4, |U|=3, 2M=4 (M=2), so possible in principle if each sunny line covers 2 points and they are disjoint, covering 4 points, but we only need 3 points, so possible. So max 2 points, insufficient for 3. So k=2 impossible for n=4 as well.

For n=3, |U|=1, 2M=2, but U has 1 point, so possible in principle; but we have only 2 sunny lines, each can cover that point? Thus k=2 seems impossible for all n≥3.

Thus k=5 likely impossible for n=6.

But we don't need to check all k; the problem asks to determine all k such that there exist n distinct lines satisfying the conditions. We have found that k=0,1,3 are always possible. We need to show that no other k is possible for any n≥3. So we need to prove that if k is not 0,1,3, then no configuration exists.

We have already argued that k cannot be 2 for any n≥3. For k=n, we argued impossible for n≥4, but for n=3, k=3 works, but k=n=3 is already included in {0,1,3}. For n=3, k=0,1,3 are possible; k=2 not. So for n=3, only 0,1,3. For n≥4, we need to prove that k cannot be any value other than 0,1,3. Possibly k could be 4 for some n? Actually, a non-sunny line could be a slope -1 line that covers many points. For n=5, the triangle has 10 points. A slope -1 line like x+y=3 covers 2 points, x+y=4 covers 3 points, x+y=5 covers 4 points, etc. Could we use a non-sunny line that covers, say, 4 points? x+y=5 covers (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1) — 4 points. But can we find 4 sunny lines that cover those 6 points? The remaining points are those not on x+y=5: a,b≥1, a+b≤6, but not sum=5. That's points: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,5),(1,6)? Actually, for n=5, points: a+b≤6. Sum=5 points: (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). So remaining: sum=2: (1,1); sum=3: (1,2),(2,1); sum=4: (1,3),(2,2),(3,1); sum=6: (1,5),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1). That's 1+2+3+5=11 points? Wait, total points=15; sum=5 points=4, so remaining=11 points. That's more than 6. I'm mixing up. For n=5, N=15. Using x+y=5 as non-sunny, we cover 4 points. Then we need 4 sunny lines to cover the remaining 11 points. But 4 sunny lines can cover at most 4*3=12 points, so it's possible in principle. But we also have to consider that the non-sunny line could be x=1 covering 5 points, leaving 10 points. Then 4 sunny lines could cover up to 12 points, so possible. But we need to see if such a covering exists. We attempted earlier and failed because the uncovered set after x=1 is the triangle with a≥2, which has 10 points, and we argued that no 4 sunny lines can cover 10 points because each sunny line covers at most 2 points from that set? Actually, we need to re-evaluate: For n=5, triangle T (a≥2,b≥1,a+b≤6) has 10 points. Could we cover these 10 points with 4 sunny lines? We can try to use lines: y=x-1 covers (2,1),(3,2),(4,3) — but (4,3) not in T? (4,3) sum=7>6, so not in T. So y=x-1 covers (2,1),(3,2) only (since (4,3) not in T). So 2 points. y=x-2 covers (3,1),(4,2) — 2 points. y=x-3 covers (4,1) — 1 point. y=2x-? (2,2) only; (3,4) not. So we have lines covering at most 2 points each. With 4 lines, max 8 points, insufficient for 10. Could we have a line covering 3 points? For instance, line through (2,1),(3,2),(4,3) but (4,3) not in T. line through (2,2),(3,3),(4,4) no. line through (2,3),(3,2),(4,1) — those three are collinear? (2,3)-(3,2): slope -1; (3,2)-(4,1): slope -1; so slope -1, not sunny. So no triple. Thus with 4 sunny lines, we can cover at most 8 points from T, leaving 2 uncovered. So k=4 impossible for n=5.

Thus, for n=5, only k=0,1,3 work.

Now, for n=6, we need to consider if k=4 might be possible? Let's attempt general reasoning: For a given n, let k be the number of sunny lines. The other n-k lines are non-sunny. Let's analyze the structure.

We can think of the triangle T_n = { (a,b) : a,b ≥ 1, a+b ≤ n+1 }. We need to cover T_n with n lines. Each line is either vertical (x=c), horizontal (y=c), or slope -1 (x+y=t). The sunny lines are all other slopes.

Observation: The points on the line x=1 are n points. To cover them, we need either a vertical line x=1 (which covers all n points), or multiple lines each covering at most one point on that line. Since we have only k sunny lines and n-k non-sunny lines, but the non-sunny lines could be horizontal or slope -1, each covering at most 1 point on x=1. So the maximum number of points on x=1 that can be covered by lines other than x=1 is (k + (n-k)) = n lines each covering 1 point, total n points. But that would require all lines to cover distinct points on x=1. However, we also have to cover the other points. So it's possible in principle to cover all n points on x=1 using a combination of sunny and non-sunny lines, each covering one point, but then each line can cover at most one point on x=1. That would mean we need n lines to cover those n points, but we only have n lines total, so if we use all lines to cover points on x=1, then we have no lines left to cover other points. Therefore, we must have x=1 among the lines to cover the n points efficiently. Similarly, for y=1, we need y=1 among the lines. So any configuration must include the lines x=1 and y=1 as non-sunny lines (since they are the only families that can cover more than one point on those vertical/horizontal lines). Therefore, n-2 non-sunny lines must include x=1 and y=1, leaving n-4 other non-sunny lines. So we have at least 2 non-sunny lines (x=1 and y=1). That forces the sunny lines to be at most n-2, and we already have k = number of sunny lines. So k ≤ n-2.

Now, consider the points on the line x+y=n+1. There are n points on that line. They need to be covered. The only line that can cover more than one point on this line is a slope -1 line, specifically x+y=n+1 itself. So to cover all n points efficiently, we either include x+y=n+1 as a line, or use other lines each covering at most one point on that line. Since we have only n lines total, and we already have x=1 and y=1, which cover (1,n) and (n,1) respectively (two points on the line x+y=n+1). The other n-2 points on that line would need to be covered by the remaining n-2 lines (including possibly the line x+y=n+1 itself). If we do not include x+y=n+1, then we would need to cover those n-2 points with the n-2 lines (some may be sunny or non-sunny). Each such line can cover at most 1 point on x+y=n+1 (since they are not slope -1). So we would need at least n-2 lines to cover those n-2 points. But we have exactly n-2 lines left, so it's possible if each covers exactly one point on that line. However, we also have to cover all other points. So it's possible that we don't use x+y=n+1 as a line. But then the points on x+y=n+1 are covered by the other lines (each covering one point). But note: those lines also cover other points. So it's not impossible a priori.

But we can use a counting argument to bound k. Let's consider the set of points with a≥2,b≥2,a+b≤n+1. Their number is T = (n-1)(n-2)/2. These points are not on x=1 or y=1. They are all in the interior triangle. Now, these points must be covered by the lines. The lines x=1 and y=1 do not cover any of them (since they have a=1 or b=1). The other non-sunny lines (besides x=1,y=1) could be horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. They can cover some of these interior points. The sunny lines can cover some of them. The number of interior points is T. The number of non-sunny lines besides x=1,y=1 is n-4. Each such non-sunny line can cover at most some number of interior points. The maximum interior points a vertical line x=c (c≥2) can cover is (n+1-c -1) = n-c points (since b from 2 to n+1-c). For c=2, that's n-2 points; c=3, n-3; etc. So the sum of interior points covered by vertical lines x=2,...,x=m is sum_{c=2}^{m} (n-c) = (n-2)+(n-3)+...+(n-m) = (m-1)n - (2+3+...+m) = (m-1)n - (m(m+1)/2 -1) = (m-1)n - (m^2+m)/2 +1. If we choose vertical lines, we can cover many interior points. But we have at most n-4 such lines, so we can choose the best ones to maximize coverage. Now we need to generalize for n≥3. Determine all k for which there exist n distinct lines covering the points (a,b) with a,b positive integers, a+b ≤ n+1, and exactly k of them are sunny.

First, note that the points are all in the first quadrant, on or below the line x+y=n+1. We need to cover them with n lines. The total number of points is N = n(n+1)/2. Each line can cover many points, but we need to see constraints on how many points can be covered by a line that is not sunny (i.e., slope 0, undefined, or -1). Also, we need to consider the possibility of using lines that are not axis-aligned but still cover many points. Perhaps we can cover all points with 3 lines (like x=1, y=1, x+y=n+1) but then we need to cover points with a>1 and b>1 and a+b<n+1? As we saw, for n=3, x+y=4 covered (1,3),(2,2),(3,1) but not (2,1) etc? Actually for n=3, x+y=4 covers (1,3),(2,2),(3,1). Then x=1 covers (1,1),(1,2),(1,3) and y=1 covers (1,1),(2,1),(3,1). So points (2,2) covered by x+y=4, (2,1) by y=1, (1,2) by x=1, (1,1) by both, (1,3) by x=1, (3,1) by y=1. So all covered. So three lines (non-sunny) suffice. For general n, can we cover all points with three lines: x=1, y=1, and x+y=n+1? Let's check. Points with a=1 are covered by x=1. Points with b=1 are covered by y=1. Points with a>1 and b>1: Their sum a+b is at least 4. Could they be on x+y=n+1? Only if a+b = n+1. But what about points with a>1,b>1 and a+b < n+1? For example, n=5: points (2,2) sum=4, (2,3) sum=5, (3,2) sum=5, (3,3) sum=6, etc. The line x+y=6 (n+1=6) covers (2,4),(3,3),(4,2) etc. But (2,2) is not covered. So three lines are not enough in general. So we need more lines.

But we have n lines. So we need to design a configuration that uses exactly n lines (distinct) to cover all points. Possibly we can use lines that are all parallel to x+y=0? That would be slope -1. But those are non-sunny. If we use many such lines, they are all non-sunny. Could we cover all points with n lines of slope -1? For each integer t from 2 to n+1, the line x+y=t passes through points (a,b) with a+b=t, a,b≥1. That's t-1 points. So the union of lines x+y=t for t=2,...,n+1 covers all points because any point (a,b) has sum a+b = s where 2≤s≤n+1, and it lies on line x+y=s. So we can take the n lines: x+y=2, x+y=3, ..., x+y=n+1. These are n lines, all parallel to x+y=0, so all non-sunny (since slope -1). They are distinct (different intercepts). So we have a set of n lines, all non-sunny, covering all points. So k=0 is always possible. (For n≥3, we have n lines, but note: For n=3, we have lines x+y=2,3,4. That works, as we saw with x+y=2 covers (1,1) only, x+y=3 covers (1,2),(2,1), x+y=4 covers (1,3),(2,2),(3,1). So indeed all points covered. So k=0 is possible for all n≥3.)

Now, can we have k=1? Need exactly one sunny line. That means we have n-1 non-sunny lines and 1 sunny line. We need to cover all points. The non-sunny lines are those with slope 0 (horizontal), undefined (vertical), or -1 (slope -1). We can choose them arbitrarily, but they must be distinct. We need to see if we can cover all points with n-1 non-sunny lines and 1 sunny line. Is there any obstruction? Possibly the sunny line must cover some points, but the non-sunny lines could cover many. Since we have n-1 non-sunny lines, we might try to use lines that are axis-aligned: x=1 and y=1, and then lines of slope -1: x+y = t for some t. But we have n-1 such lines; they could be chosen as the lines x=1, y=1, and x+y=2,3,...,n? That would be n lines? Actually x=1, y=1, and x+y=2,...,n gives total: 1+1+(n-1)= n+1 lines, too many. We only have n-1 non-sunny lines. So we need to choose a set of n-1 non-sunny lines that together with the sunny line cover all points.

We need to find for which n (≥3) there exists a configuration with exactly 1 sunny line. Possibly for all n? Let's attempt to construct.

Idea: Use the sunny line to cover a set of points that would otherwise require many lines, and use the remaining non-sunny lines to cover the rest. Since we have many non-sunny lines, we can use lines like x=1 and y=1 (2 lines) and then lines of slope -1: x+y = t for t = 2,3,...,n? That would be n-2 lines? Let's count: x=1, y=1, and x+y=2,...,n gives: 2 + (n-1) = n+1 lines, too many. But we only need n-1 non-sunny lines. So we could drop some lines. For instance, we could use x=1, y=1, and then x+y=2,...,n-1? That gives 2 + (n-2) = n lines. But we need n-1 non-sunny lines. So we have one extra line. Possibly we can use x=1 and then lines of slope -1 for t=2,...,n? That would be 1 + (n-1) = n lines, all non-sunny. But then we need a sunny line. So we need to replace some non-sunny lines with sunny line? Actually we need exactly 1 sunny line, so the rest are non-sunny. We have n lines total. So we need to choose n-1 non-sunny lines and 1 sunny line. The non-sunny lines could be chosen as a set that covers all points except maybe some that are covered by the sunny line. So the sunny line might be used to cover some points that are not covered by the non-sunny lines.

We need to see if we can cover all points with n-1 lines of slope 0,∞,-1 and one line of another slope. Possibly we can use lines: x=1, y=1, and then lines of slope -1: x+y = t for t = 2,3,...,n-1? That's 1+1+(n-2)= n lines, all non-sunny. But we need exactly 1 sunny line, so we cannot have all non-sunny. So we need to replace some of these non-sunny lines with a sunny line. But if we replace a line, we still need to cover its points. Perhaps the sunny line could cover many points that were previously covered by multiple non-sunny lines, and then we can drop some non-sunny lines to reduce the count to n-1, and add the sunny line. So we need to find a set of n-1 non-sunny lines (maybe including x=1, y=1, and some x+y=t) and a sunny line such that their union covers all points.

Let's try to design for general n.

First, consider the set of points: (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b ≤ n+1. We can try to use the following lines:
- L0: x=1 (vertical, non-sunny). This covers all points with a=1, b from 1 to n.
- L1: y=1 (horizontal, non-sunny). This covers all points with b=1, a from 1 to n.
- For the remaining points (a,b) with a≥2, b≥2, and a+b ≤ n+1, we need to cover them with the remaining n-2 non-sunny lines (since we have n-1 non-sunny total, 2 already used) and 1 sunny line.

How many such points are there? Points with a≥2,b≥2 and a+b ≤ n+1. For a=2, b from 2 to n-1; a=3, b from 2 to n-2; ... up to a=n-1, b=2; and also a=n,b=1? No b≥2, so a from 2 to n-1? Actually a=n, b=1 not allowed because b≥2; so a from 2 to n-1. Also a=n? a=n,b=1 not; a=n-1,b=2 allowed; a=n-1,b=1 not; so the number of such points: For a=2, b=2,...,n-1 gives (n-2) points; a=3, b=2,...,n-2 gives (n-3) points; ... a=n-1, b=2 gives 1 point. In general, a line with slope p/q (reduced) can pass through integer points with x increasing by q, y increasing by p. For points (a,b) with a,b positive and a+b ≤ n+1, the x-coordinate can increase by q, but the sum a+b would increase by q+p. Since a+b ≤ n+1, the number of such points on the line is at most floor((n+1)/(q+p))? Actually, if the line passes through integer points, the points are of the form (x0 + kq, y0 + kp). The sum (x+y) = (x0+y0) + k(p+q). For this to be ≤ n+1, k can be at most floor((n+1 - (x0+y0))/(p+q)). So the maximum number of lattice points on the line within the triangle is limited. Let's try to see if k=2 is ever possible. For n=3, we argued no. For n=4, we argued no. For n=5, we argued no. So perhaps k=2 is never possible. But we need to be systematic.

We need to find all possible k for a given n. The construction for k=0 and k=1 always works. For k=n? We need to check if there exists a set of n sunny lines covering all points. Since we have n lines, all sunny. The points are N = n(n+1)/2. Each sunny line can cover at most some number of points. For a sunny line, what is the maximum number of lattice points from the triangle it can contain? The line has slope not 0,∞,-1. For a line with slope p/q in reduced form, the integer points are spaced by (q,p). The sum x+y increases by q+p along the line. So if the line passes through a point (x0,y0), then the points are (x0+kq, y0+kp). The sum is (x0+y0)+k(q+p). For these to be ≤ n+1, we need k ≤ (n+1 - (x0+y0))/(q+p). The number of points on the line within the triangle is at most floor((n+1)/(q+p))? Actually, if we start from a point with small sum, we can have many points. For slope 1 (p=q=1), then q+p=2, so we can have points with sums 2,4,6,... up to n+1 (if n+1 even) or n (if n+1 odd). So number of points on y=x is floor((n+1)/2). For slope 2 (p=2,q=1), q+p=3, so we can have points with sums 3,6,9,... up to n+1, so number = floor((n+1)/3)? Actually, if we start at (1,2) sum=3, then next (2,4) sum=6, then (3,6) sum=9, etc. So number = floor((n+1)/3)? But (n+1)/3 may not be integer, but the points are on the line, and we need to ensure that the x,y are positive. For slope 2, the points are (a,2a) for a≥1. The sum = 3a ≤ n+1 => a ≤ floor((n+1)/3). So number = floor((n+1)/3). Similarly, for slope 3, number = floor((n+1)/4). For slope 1/2, points (2a,a) sum=3a ≤ n+1 => a ≤ floor((n+1)/3) gives number = floor((n+1)/3) as well. So the maximum number of points on a sunny line within the triangle is floor((n+1)/2) for slope 1. For slope 1, it's the largest possible because 2 is the smallest sum increase? Actually, slope 1 gives q+p=2, so number of points can be as large as floor((n+1)/2). For slope 2, q+p=3, so number = floor((n+1)/3) which is less than floor((n+1)/2) for n≥3. So the maximum points per sunny line is M = floor((n+1)/2). For n odd, n+1 even, so M = (n+1)/2. For n even, n+1 odd, so M = n/2.

Now, if we have all n lines sunny, each can cover at most M points. The total points needed is N = n(n+1)/2. So the maximum total coverage if all lines are sunny is n * M. For this to be at least N, we need n * M ≥ n(n+1)/2 => M ≥ (n+1)/2. For n odd, M = (n+1)/2, so equality holds: n * (n+1)/2 = N. For n even, M = n/2, so n * n/2 = n^2/2, but N = n(n+1)/2 = (n^2+n)/2, which is greater than n^2/2 for n≥1. So for even n, n * M < N. Therefore, it's impossible to have all n lines sunny when n is even because even if each sunny line covers the maximum possible points (M), the total coverage is less than the number of points needed. However, could we have some sunny lines covering more than M? M is the maximum possible for any sunny line within the triangle. So no. So for even n, k=n is impossible. For odd n, it's possible in principle if we can achieve the maximum coverage with each sunny line covering exactly M points and all points disjoint. So only c=0 gives M points. So the only sunny line that can cover M points is y=x. Now, what about k=2? Could it be possible for some n? For k=2, we have 2 sunny lines and n-2 non-sunny lines. We need to cover all points. The non-sunny lines are at most n-2 lines. Actually careful: Points with a=1: b from 1 to n; that's n points; a=2: b from 1 to n-1; ... a=n-2: b from 1 to 3? Wait, a+b≤n+1, so for a=n-2, b≤3. But we also have non-sunny lines that could be slope -1 lines, etc. The maximum coverage from n-2 non-sunny lines is when they are all vertical (since each vertical line covers many points, but we need to consider that the union of vertical lines x=1..n-2 covers points with a from 1 to n-2, leaving points with a=n-1 and a=n (and possibly some b). The number of points not covered by these vertical lines is points with a≥n-1. Those are: a=n-1: b from 1 to 2 (since n-1+b≤n+1 => b≤2); a=n: b=1; total = 2+1=3 points. So if we use vertical lines x=1..x=n-2, we cover (n-2)(n+3)/2 points, leaving 3 points uncovered. Then we have 2 sunny lines to cover these 3 points. As before, each sunny line can cover at most 1 point if the points are such that any line through two of them is non-sunny? The three points are (n-1,1), (n-1,2), (n,1). Check pairs: (n-1,1)-(n-1,2) vertical, not sunny; (n-1,1)-(n,1) horizontal, not sunny; (n-1,2)-(n,1) slope -1, not sunny. So no sunny pair. So each sunny line can cover at most 1 point. So we need at least 3 sunny lines to cover 3 points. So k=2 insufficient.

What if we use a different set of non-sunny lines that cover more points, leaving fewer than 3 points uncovered? For k=2, we have n-2 non-sunny lines. Their total coverage could be larger if we use lines of slope -1? For instance, if we use three slope -1 lines, they cover points with sum 2,3,4, which is 6 points; but then we have n-2 lines, we can use many vertical lines plus some slope -1. But the total number of points is N. To have only 2 sunny lines cover the rest, we need the uncovered points to be at most 2*M where M is max points per sunny line? Actually, we need to cover the uncovered points with 2 sunny lines. Since each sunny line can cover at most M points, the maximum number of points they can cover is 2M. So we need the uncovered points ≤ 2M. But also, we need the union of non-sunny lines to cover the rest. So we need to find a set of n-2 non-sunny lines that covers at least N - 2M points. Since N = n(n+1)/2, and M = floor((n+1)/2), we have 2M = 2 floor((n+1)/2). For n odd, n+1 even, so M = (n+1)/2, 2M = n+1. So N - 2M = n(n+1)/2 - (n+1) = (n-2)(n+1)/2. That's the number of points we need to cover with non-sunny lines. For n=5, that's (3*6)/2=9; non-sunny lines = n-2=3; can 3 non-sunny lines cover 9 points? Possibly if they are vertical lines x=1,2,3: they cover 5+4+3=12 points, which is >9; so we could have more than enough coverage, but we also need to cover the remaining points with 2 sunny lines. However, the uncovered points after non-sunny lines are N - coverage of non-sunny. If we use vertical lines x=1..x=3, they cover 12 points, leaving 15-12=3 points; 2 sunny lines need to cover 3 points, but as before they can only cover at most 2 points if a sunny line can cover 2 of them? Actually, for n=5, M=3, but the three points left are (4,1),(4,2),(5,1). A sunny line can cover at most 2 of them? Check pairs: (4,1)-(4,2) vertical, not sunny; (4,1)-(5,1) horizontal, not sunny; (4,2)-(5,1) slope -1, not sunny. So no sunny pair, each sunny line covers at most 1 point. So 2 sunny lines cover at most 2 points, leaving 1 uncovered. So not. Could we choose non-sunny lines differently so that the uncovered set is a set of points that can be covered by 2 sunny lines? For n=5, we need to cover 15 points with 3 non-sunny and 2 sunny. Let's attempt to see if there is any configuration with 2 sunny lines that covers all 15 points. We already found a configuration with k=3 (3 sunny lines) works. For k=2, we need 2 sunny lines. Suppose we try to use x=1 and y=1 as non-sunny lines, covering 9 points (as computed). Then uncovered: (2,2),(2,3),(2,4),(3,2),(3,3),(4,2). That's 6 points. Can we cover these with 2 sunny lines? Each sunny line can cover at most 3 points (M=3). So we need two lines that cover 6 points. Possible if each covers 3 points. But can we find two sunny lines that each have 3 points within that uncovered set? The uncovered set: points with a=2,b=2,3,4; a=3,b=2,3; a=4,b=2. That's 6 points. Is there a sunny line that contains 3 of these? Consider line y=x: points (2,2),(3,3) — only 2 from set; (1,1) not in set. So 2 points. y=2x: (2,4) only one; y=x+1: (2,3),(3,4) but (3,4) not in set; so 2 points. y=x+2: (2,4),(3,5) no; so 2 points. y=2x-1: (2,3) only; y=2x-2: (2,2) only; y=3x-4: (2,2) only; etc. So no line with 3 points. Could a line have 3 points? For instance, line through (2,2),(3,2),(4,2) horizontal not sunny. Through (2,2),(2,3),(2,4) vertical not sunny. Through (2,3),(3,2),(4,1) no. So max 2 points per sunny line. So with 2 sunny lines, max points covered = 4, but we have 6 points. So impossible.

What if we use vertical lines x=1,2,3 as non-sunny? Uncovered: (4,1),(4,2),(5,1). 3 points. As argued, no sunny pair, so 2 sunny lines can cover at most 2 points. So fail.

What if we use non-sunny lines: x=1, x+y=3, x+y=4? For n=5, that's 3 non-sunny lines. Coverage: x=1: 5 points; x+y=3: (1,2),(2,1) — but (1,2) already covered, so new (2,1); x+y=4: (1,3),(2,2),(3,1) — new (2,2),(3,1). So total covered: (1,1)-(1,5); (2,1); (2,2); (3,1). That's 5+1+1+1=8 points? Actually (1,1)-(1,5):5; (2,1):1; (2,2):1; (3,1):1; total 8. Uncovered: (2,3),(2,4),(3,2),(3,3),(4,1),(4,2),(5,1). That's 7 points. Too many.

Thus k=2 seems impossible for n=5.

General approach: Determine all k for which there exists a set of n lines covering the points. We have a construction for k=0 and k=1 always. For k=n, we have n=3 possible, n≥4 impossible. For k=n-1? Possibly for some n? Let's test n=4, k=3 works (we found). For n=5, k=4? We argued impossible because total points 15, 4 sunny lines max coverage = 4*M = 4*3=12, need 15, but we have 1 non-sunny line covering at most 5 points (if vertical) so total max coverage = 5+12=17, but we need to cover all 15 points; the issue is that the union of 4 sunny lines and 1 non-sunny line might cover 15 points if the non-sunny line covers some points and the sunny lines cover the rest. But we need to see if there exists a configuration with 4 sunny lines and 1 non-sunny line covering all points. For n=5, we need 1 non-sunny line that covers some points, and 4 sunny lines cover the rest. Let's attempt to design. We already have a configuration with k=3 (3 sunny lines) works. For k=4, we can try to use x=1 as non-sunny, covering 5 points. Then we need 4 sunny lines to cover the remaining 10 points. As we argued, each sunny line can cover at most 3 points, but 4*3=12, so possible if we can cover exactly 10 points with 4 sunny lines (some covering 2 points, some 3). However, we need to see if there exists a set of 4 sunny lines that together cover the 10 points (2,1)-(2,4); (3,1)-(3,3); (4,1)-(4,2); (5,1). Can we cover these 10 points with 4 sunny lines? Let's attempt to find a partition. The points: a=2: b=1,2,3,4 (4 points); a=3: b=1,2,3 (3 points); a=4: b=1,2 (2 points); a=5: b=1 (1 point). Total 10. We need 4 lines. Suppose we use y=x covering (2,2),(3,3) — 2 new points. Then left 8 points. Need 3 sunny lines to cover 8 points, average 2.67. Could we have a line with 3 points? For instance, line through (2,1),(3,2),(4,3) but (4,3) not in set; (2,4),(3,3),(4,2) are on x+y=6 slope -1 not sunny; (2,1),(3,2) only 2; (2,3),(3,2) slope -1; (2,4),(3,3) slope 1? Actually (2,4) to (3,3) slope -1, not sunny. So no triple. So max 2 points per sunny line. With 3 sunny lines, max 6 points; we need 8, impossible. So maybe we can use a different non-sunny line. For k=4, we have 1 non-sunny line. Could it be a slope -1 line? For instance, x+y=2 (non-sunny) covers (1,1) only. Then we need 4 sunny lines to cover the remaining 14 points. Each sunny line max 3 points, total max 12, insufficient. So that fails.

What about using x+y=3 (non-sunny) covering (1,2),(2,1) — 2 points. Then need 4 sunny lines to cover 13 points, max 12, fails. x+y=4 covers 3 points; then need 12 points, possible if each sunny line covers 3 points, but only y=x can cover 3 points; others at most 2. So we would need 4 lines each covering 3 points, but only one line can achieve 3 points. So impossible. x+y=5 covers 4 points; then need 11 points, 4 sunny lines max 12, possible but need to cover 11 points. But is there a set of 4 sunny lines that cover the 11 points? The uncovered points would be all points with sum ≤6 except those on x+y=5. That set includes (1,1),(1,3),(1,4),(1,5); (2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(2,4); (3,1),(3,2),(3,3); (4,1),(4,2); (5,1). That's many. But we have only 4 sunny lines; they can cover at most 4*3=12 points, so maybe we could cover 11 points if we use y=x (3 points), and three other lines each covering 2 points (total 3+6=9), still need 2 more points; so we need to have some lines covering more than 2 points? Only y=x gives 3. So with 4 lines, max points covered = 3+2+2+2=9, not 11. So no.

Thus k=4 impossible for n=5.

For n=4, we found k=3 works. What about k=2? We tried and failed. So for n=4, possible k: 0,1,3. For n=5, possible k: 0,1,3? We haven't found k=2,4,5. k=3 works, k=0,1 works. So pattern: k can be 0,1, and possibly also n when n=3? For n=3, k=3 works; for n=4, k=3 works; for n=5, k=3 works; so k=3 works for n=3,4,5? For n=3, k=3 works; n=4, k=3 works; n=5, k=3 works; so maybe k=3 works for all n≥3? But we need to check n=6: can we have k=3? That would be 3 sunny lines and 3 non-sunny lines (since n=6). We need to see if we can cover all 21 points with 3 non-sunny lines and 3 sunny lines. We can try to use x=1, y=1 as non-sunny (2 lines) covering 11 points? Actually for n=6, points: a+b≤7. x=1 covers (1,1)-(1,6) = 6 points. y=1 covers (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(5,1),(6,1) = 6 points. Overlap (1,1) gives total 11 points. Then uncovered: (2,2),(2,3),(2,4),(2,5); (3,2),(3,3),(3,4); (4,2),(4,3); (5,2); (6,1) already covered by y=1? Actually (6,1) is on y=1, so covered. So uncovered: points with a=2,b=2..5 (4 points); a=3,b=2..4 (3 points); a=4,b=2..3 (2 points); a=5,b=2 (1 point); total 4+3+2+1=10 points. We have 3 sunny lines to cover these 10 points. Each sunny line can cover at most M = floor((n+1)/2) = floor(7/2)=3 points. So 3 sunny lines max 9 points, but we need 10. So k=3 impossible for n=6. Could we use different non-sunny lines? For n=6, we have 3 non-sunny lines. Maybe we can use three vertical lines x=1,2,3 covering 6+5+4=15 points? Then uncovered: a=4: b from 1 to 3? Actually a=4, b≤3 (since 4+b≤7 => b≤3) gives 3 points; a=5: b≤2 gives 2 points; a=6: b≤1 gives 1 point; total 6 points. We have 3 sunny lines to cover 6 points. Since each sunny line max 3 points, we could possibly cover exactly 6 points if each sunny line covers 2 points. But can we find 3 sunny lines that cover these 6 points? The points: (4,1),(4,2),(4,3); (5,1),(5,2); (6,1). That's 6 points. We need to partition into 3 sunny pairs. Check sunny pairs: (4,1)-(5,1) horizontal, not sunny; (4,2)-(5,2) horizontal; (4,3)-(5,3) but (5,3) not in set (sum=8>7); (4,1)-(6,1) horizontal; (4,2)-(6,2) not; (4,3)-(6,3) not; (5,1)-(6,1) horizontal; (5,2)-(6,2) not; (4,1)-(5,2) slope (2-1)/(5-4)=1, sunny! So line through (4,1) and (5,2): slope 1, equation y=x-3? For x=4,y=1; x=5,y=2; so y=x-3. That line also passes through (6,3) not in set, so covers (4,1),(5,2). (4,2)-(5,1): slope (1-2)/(5-4)=-1, not sunny. (4,3)-(5,2): slope (2-3)/(5-4)=-1, not sunny. (4,3)-(6,1): slope (1-3)/(6-4)= -2/2=-1, not sunny. (4,2)-(6,2): not. (5,1)-(6,1) horizontal. (5,2)-(6,1): slope (1-2)/(6-5)=-1, not sunny. So we have one sunny pair: (4,1)-(5,2). Then left (4,2),(4,3),(5,1),(6,1). Could we cover these with 2 sunny lines? For instance, (4,2)-(5,1): slope -1 not sunny; (4,3)-(5,1): slope -2, sunny? Check (4,3) to (5,1): Δy=-2, Δx=1, slope -2, sunny. So line through (4,3) and (5,1) is sunny: y-3 = -2(x-4) => y = -2x+11. That covers (4,3) and (5,1). Then left (4,2) and (6,1). Could we cover these with a sunny line? (4,2)-(6,1): slope (1-2)/(6-4)= -1/2, sunny. So line through (4,2) and (6,1): y-2 = -1/2(x-4) => y = -0.5x+4. That covers (4,2) and (6,1). So we have three sunny lines: L1: y=x-3 (through (4,1),(5,2)); L2: y=-2x+11 (through (4,3),(5,1)); L3: y=-0.5x+4 (through (4,2),(6,1)). These are sunny (slopes 1, -2, -1/2). So this configuration works: non-sunny lines: x=1, x=2, x=3 (vertical), sunny lines: y=x-3, y=-2x+11, y=-0.5x+4. Check coverage: x=1 covers (1,1)-(1,6); x=2 covers (2,1)-(2,5); x=3 covers (3,1)-(3,4). That's 6+5+4=15 points. Then sunny lines cover: (4,1),(5,2) from y=x-3; (4,3),(5,1) from y=-2x+11; (4,2),(6,1) from y=-0.5x+4. So all points covered: (1,1)-(1,6); (2,1)-(2,5); (3,1)-(3,4); (4,1)-(4,3); (5,1)-(5,2); (6,1). That's all points? Points with a=5,b=3? Not in set because sum=8>7. So yes. So k=3 works for n=6. But earlier we thought k=3 impossible for n=6 because using x=1,y=1 gave 10 uncovered points and 3 sunny lines max 9 points. However, we found a configuration with 3 vertical lines and 3 sunny lines that covers all 21 points. So k=3 is possible for n=6. So it's not that k=3 only works for odd n. So we have a construction for k=3 for n=6.

Now, for n=6, can we have k=2? Let's test. We need 2 sunny lines and 4 non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines could be vertical lines x=1,2,3,4 (cover points with a=1..4). That's 6+5+4+3=18 points? Actually for n=6, points: a+b≤7. Vertical lines x=1: b=1..6 (6 points); x=2: b=1..5 (5); x=3: b=1..4 (4); x=4: b=1..3 (3). Total = 6+5+4+3=18 points. Check pairs: (5,1)-(5,2) vertical, not sunny; (5,1)-(6,1) horizontal, not sunny; (5,2)-(6,1) slope -1, not sunny. So no sunny pair, each sunny line can cover at most 1 point. So 2 sunny lines cover at most 2 points, leaving 1 uncovered. So fail.

Could we use a different set of 4 non-sunny lines? Maybe we can use three vertical lines and one slope -1 line that covers many points, leaving fewer points uncovered? Could there be a line with 3 points? Check possible lines: y=x? Points (4,4) not in set; (5,5) no; so 2 points max. y=x-3? Points (4,1),(5,2) only 2; (4,2),(5,3) not; (4,3),(5,4) no; so 2. y=2x? (4,8) no; (5,10) no; so 1. y=2x-? (4,? ) Not. Consider line through (4,1),(5,1),(6,1) horizontal, not sunny. Through (4,1),(4,2),(4,3) vertical, not. Through (4,2),(5,1),(6,0) not. So no line with 3 points. Could a line have 3 points if we include points from other a? For instance, line through (4,1),(5,2),(6,3) but (6,3) sum=9>7, not in set. So no. So max 2 points per sunny line, total max 4, insufficient. So that fails.

What if we use 4 non-sunny lines that are slope -1 lines? x+y=2,3,4,5? That covers points with sum 2..5, total 1+2+3+4=10 points. Uncovered: sum=6,7: points (1,5),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1) (sum=6) and (1,6),(2,5),(3,4),(4,3),(5,2),(6,1) (sum=7). That's 5+6=11 points. 2 sunny lines can cover at most 6 points, insufficient.

Thus k=2 seems impossible for n=6.

Now n=7, we already considered k=7 impossible. Could k=3? Let's try to construct for n=7 with k=3. We need 3 sunny lines and 4 non-sunny lines. We can try to use vertical lines x=1,2,3,4 (cover a=1..4). For n=7, points: a+b≤8. Vertical lines: x=1: b=1..7 (7 points); x=2: b=1..6 (6); x=3: b=1..5 (5); x=4: b=1..4 (4). Total = 7+6+5+4=22 points. Uncovered: a=5: b=1..3 (3 points); a=6: b=1..2 (2); a=7: b=1 (1); total 6 points. With 3 sunny lines, each max M = floor((7+1)/2)=4 points. So 3*4=12, enough. Need to cover 6 points with 3 sunny lines. Possible if each sunny line covers 2 points, or some cover 3. Let's see if we can partition the 6 points into 3 sunny pairs. Points: (5,1),(5,2),(5,3); (6,1),(6,2); (7,1). Check sunny pairs: (5,1)-(6,2): slope (2-1)/(6-5)=1, sunny? Actually (5,1) to (6,2) slope 1, line y=x-4. That covers (5,1),(6,2). (5,2)-(6,1): slope (1-2)/(6-5)=-1, not sunny. (5,3)-(6,2): slope (2-3)/(6-5)=-1, not sunny. (5,1)-(7,1): horizontal, not sunny. (5,2)-(7,2) not; (5,3)-(7,3) not; (6,1)-(7,1): horizontal; (6,2)-(7,1): slope -1, not sunny. (5,1)-(5,2) vertical; (5,2)-(5,3) vertical; (6,1)-(6,2) vertical. So only one sunny pair: (5,1)-(6,2).
