Then left (5,3) and (6,1). Check (5,3)-(6,1): slope (1-3)/(6-5)=-2, sunny! So line through (5,3) and (6,1) is sunny: y-3 = -2(x-5) => y = -2x+13. So we have two sunny lines covering those pairs. So we have three sunny lines: L1: y=x-4 (through (5,1),(6,2)); L2: y=-x/2+4.5 (through (5,2),(7,1)); L3: y=-2x+13 (through (5,3),(6,1)). These are all sunny. So this configuration works: non-sunny lines: x=1,2,3,4 (vertical), sunny lines as above. So k=3 possible for n=7.

Thus k=3 seems possible for all n≥3? For n=3,4,5,6,7 we have found constructions. Let's test n=8: can we do k=3? We need 3 sunny lines and 5 non-sunny lines. Use vertical lines x=1..5? For n=8, points: a+b≤9. Vertical lines x=1: b=1..8 (8); x=2:7; x=3:6; x=4:5; x=5:4; total = 8+7+6+5+4=30 points. Uncovered: a=6: b=1..3 (3); a=7: b=1..2 (2); a=8: b=1 (1); total 6 points. 3 sunny lines can cover 6 points if each covers 2 points. The uncovered points: (6,1),(6,2),(6,3); (7,1),(7,2); (8,1). We need to partition into 3 sunny pairs. Check sunny pairs: (6,1)-(7,2): slope 1, line y=x-5? Actually (6,1) to (7,2): slope 1, equation y=x-5. That covers (6,1),(7,2). (6,2)-(7,1): slope -1, not sunny. (6,3)-(7,2): slope -1, not. (6,1)-(8,1): horizontal, not. (6,2)-(8,2) not; (6,3)-(8,3) not; (7,1)-(8,1): horizontal; (7,2)-(8,1): slope -1; (6,1)-(6,2) vertical; (6,2)-(6,3) vertical; (7,1)-(7,2) vertical. So only one sunny pair: (6,1)-(7,2). Left (6,2),(6,3),(7,1),(8,1). Need 2 sunny lines to cover these 4 points. Check (6,3)-(7,1): slope -2, sunny! So line through (6,3) and (7,1): y-3 = -2(x-6) => y = -2x+15. So we have three sunny lines: L1: y=x-5; L2: y=-x/2+5; L3: y=-2x+15. All sunny. So k=3 works for n=8.

Thus it seems k=3 is always possible for n≥3. But we need to verify for n=3,4,5,6,7,8 all work. So we have a construction for k=3 for any n≥3: Take the n-3 vertical lines x=1,2,...,n-3 (non-sunny). Then the uncovered points are those with a = n-2, n-1, n? Let's compute general. For n≥3, we have points (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b≤n+1. If we take vertical lines x=1,...,x=n-3, they cover all points with a from 1 to n-3. The remaining points have a = n-2, n-1, n. For a = n-2, b can be from 1 to 3 (since n-2+b≤n+1 => b≤3). For a = n-1, b can be from 1 to 2 (since n-1+b≤n+1 => b≤2). For a = n, b=1. So total uncovered points = 3+2+1=6 points. We need to cover these 6 points with 3 sunny lines (since total lines = n, we have n-3 non-sunny lines, so we need n-(n-3)=3 sunny lines). So we need to partition these 6 points into 3 pairs, each pair lying on a sunny line. As we did for n=6,7,8, we found such a partition. Let's verify that for general n≥3, the uncovered set U = { (n-2,1), (n-2,2), (n-2,3), (n-1,1), (n-1,2), (n,1) }. We need to find 3 disjoint pairs such that each pair's line has slope not 0,∞,-1. Check all possible sunny lines through these points. Consider the line through (n-2,1) and (n-1,2): slope = (2-1)/(n-1-(n-2)) = 1/1 =1, sunny. That's one pair. Then line through (n-2,2) and (n,1): slope = (1-2)/(n-(n-2)) = -1/2, sunny. Then line through (n-2,3) and (n-1,1): slope = (1-3)/(n-1-(n-2)) = -2/1 = -2, sunny. So these three pairs are disjoint and cover all 6 points. Indeed, (n-2,1)-(n-1,2); (n-2,2)-(n,1); (n-2,3)-(n-1,1). Are these lines distinct? Yes, slopes 1, -1/2, -2. So they are sunny. So for any n≥3, this works. Therefore k=3 is always possible.

Now, what about k=2? Could it be possible for some n? For k=2, we have 2 sunny lines and n-2 non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines are at most n-2. We need to cover all points. The maximum number of points we can cover with n-2 non-sunny lines is when we use vertical lines x=1,...,x=n-2? That covers points with a=1..n-2, total = sum_{a=1}^{n-2} (n+1-a) = sum_{j=3}^{n} j = (n+3)(n-2)/2. Then uncovered points are those with a=n-1,n: (n-1,1),(n-1,2),(n,1) — 3 points. Thus k=2 seems impossible for n=8.

Therefore, it's plausible that k=2 is never possible. But we must be thorough: Is there any n for which k=2 is possible? Let's attempt a general argument.

We have n lines, exactly k sunny. The other n-k lines are non-sunny (parallel to axes or x+y=0). We need to cover all points (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b≤n+1.

Consider the points on the line x=1 (vertical axis). There are n points (1,1) to (1,n). Similarly, points on y=1: (1,1) to (n,1). Also points on x+y=n+1: (1,n), (2,n-1), ..., (n,1). These are n points. The set of all points is N = n(n+1)/2.

We need to cover them with n lines. Let's analyze constraints on the number of non-sunny lines. A non-sunny line is either horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. The points on these lines are limited. For instance, a horizontal line y=c (c≥1) covers points with b=c, a from 1 to n+1-c. So it covers n+1-c points. A vertical line x=c covers points with a=c, b from 1 to n+1-c. So it covers n+1-c points. A slope -1 line x+y=t (t≥2) covers points with sum t, a from 1 to t-1, b=t-a, so it covers t-1 points.

Now, note that the total number of points is N = n(n+1)/2. The sum of the numbers of points on each line is at most the sum of the points covered by the lines, but points can be covered by multiple lines; we need the union to cover all points. So we need to select lines such that their union is the whole triangle.

We want to determine possible k (number of sunny lines). We have constructions for k=0,1,3 always. For k=n, we have n=3 possible, but n≥4 impossible. Could k=2 be possible? Let's try to prove it's impossible for all n≥3.

Assume k=2, so we have 2 sunny lines and n-2 non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines can be of three families. Let's denote the set of points not on the sunny lines. Since the sunny lines cover some points, we need the non-sunny lines to cover the rest. But we need to cover all points, so the union of the non-sunny lines must cover the points not covered by the sunny lines. However, the sunny lines can also cover some points that are also on non-sunny lines; that's okay.

We can try to use a counting argument: For a given configuration, consider the points on the line x=1. They are n points. These points must be covered by some lines. The sunny lines are not vertical, so they cannot be x=1; they could pass through some (1,b). Since sunny lines have slopes not 0,∞,-1, they could pass through at most one point on x=1? Actually, a line through (1,b) with slope s can intersect x=1 at only that point. So each sunny line can cover at most one point on x=1 (if it passes through that point). Similarly, a horizontal line y=c can cover at most one point on x=1 (if c equals b). A vertical line x=1 covers all n points on x=1. A slope -1 line x+y=t can cover at most one point on x=1 (if t=1+b, then it passes through (1,b)). So the only line that can cover more than one point on x=1 is the vertical line x=1 itself. So if we want to cover all n points on x=1, we must either include x=1 as a line, or have many sunny lines each covering one point on x=1, but we only have 2 sunny lines, so they can cover at most 2 points on x=1. The other points on x=1 would then need to be covered by non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines can be horizontal or slope -1, but they can cover at most 1 point on x=1 each (since they are not vertical). So to cover n points on x=1, we need at least n-2 non-sunny lines that cover points on x=1, but we only have n-2 non-sunny lines total. So we need to use x=1 as one of the non-sunny lines to cover all n points. Therefore, x=1 must be among the n-2 non-sunny lines. Similarly, we can argue for y=1: the points on y=1 are n points, and the only line that can cover more than one point on y=1 is y=1 itself (horizontal). So y=1 must also be among the non-sunny lines. However, we only have n-2 non-sunny lines, and we need both x=1 and y=1 to be non-sunny lines. That's fine; they can be two of them. But then we also have other non-sunny lines. So the non-sunny lines include at least x=1 and y=1. That uses 2 lines. Then we have n-4 other non-sunny lines (since total non-sunny = n-2). But we also have 2 sunny lines. So total lines = 2 (sunny) + 2 (axis) + (n-4) others = n. Good.

Now, consider the points on the line x+y=n+1. There are n points (1,n),(2,n-1),...,(n,1). These points are all in the triangle. They must be covered by lines. The sunny lines cannot be slope -1, so they could pass through some of these points. The only non-sunny lines that can cover many points on this line are slope -1 lines themselves, but the line x+y=n+1 is slope -1, so if we include it as a line, it covers all n points. So we need to either include x+y=n+1 as a non-sunny line, or cover its points with other lines. But we already have x=1 and y=1 as non-sunny lines, which cover (1,n) and (n,1) respectively (since (1,n) is on x=1, (n,1) on y=1). The other points on x+y=n+1 are (2,n-1),...,(n-1,2). These are n-2 points. They could be covered by sunny lines or by other non-sunny lines. However, a sunny line can cover at most 1 point on this line? Actually, a sunny line has slope not -1, so it can intersect the line x+y=n+1 at most once (since two lines with different slopes intersect at most once). So each sunny line can cover at most 1 point on x+y=n+1. With 2 sunny lines, they can cover at most 2 points. The other n-4 points would then need to be covered by non-sunny lines. Could they be covered by vertical or horizontal lines? For instance, the point (2,n-1) has a=2, b=n-1. Could it be on a vertical line x=2? Yes, if we include x=2 as a non-sunny line. That would cover (2,n-1) (and also other points). Similarly, horizontal line y=n-1 would cover (2,n-1) (if we include y=n-1). But we only have n-4 non-sunny lines besides x=1 and y=1. Could we include all vertical lines x=2,...,n-2? That would be n-3 lines, too many. So we need to be clever.

Alternatively, we might choose to include the line x+y=n+1 as one of the non-sunny lines. Then it covers all n points. That uses one line. So we have non-sunny lines: x=1, y=1, and x+y=n+1. That's 3 lines. Then we have n-5 other non-sunny lines? Actually total non-sunny = n-2. So we have 3 used, leaving n-5 non-sunny lines to choose. Also we have 2 sunny lines. So total lines = 2+3+(n-5)=n. Good.

Now, consider the points not on these three lines. Which points are not covered? The union of x=1, y=1, and x+y=n+1 covers: all points with a=1 (n points), all points with b=1 (n points), and all points with sum=n+1 (n points). But there is overlap: (1,1) is on both x=1 and y=1; (1,n) on x=1 and sum=n+1; (n,1) on y=1 and sum=n+1. So total covered points = n + n + n - 3 = 3n - 3? Actually, x=1 covers n points; y=1 covers n points, but (1,1) counted twice; x+y=n+1 covers n points, but (1,n) and (n,1) already counted in x=1 and y=1 respectively, so overlaps: (1,1) with x=1,y=1; (1,n) with x=1; (n,1) with y=1; and also (1,1) is not on sum=n+1? (1,1) sum=2, not; (1,n) sum=n+1, (n,1) sum=n+1; so total distinct points = n + n + n - (1 for (1,1) double counted) - (1 for (1,n) double counted) - (1 for (n,1) double counted) = 3n - 3. That's the number of points covered by these three lines. The total number of points is N = n(n+1)/2. So the uncovered points are N - (3n-3). For n≥3, N = (n^2+n)/2, so uncovered = (n^2+n)/2 - 3n + 3 = (n^2+n-6n+6)/2 = (n^2-5n+6)/2 = (n-2)(n-3)/2. That's the number of points with a≥2, b≥2, and a+b≤n+1? Actually, points with a≥2,b≥2, and a+b≤n+1 are exactly those not on x=1 or y=1 or sum=n+1? But sum=n+1 includes some with a,b≥2? For a,b≥2, a+b≤n+1, the points on sum=n+1 are those with a+b=n+1, which includes points like (2,n-1) etc. So uncovered points are those with a,b≥2, a+b≤n+1, but not on sum=n+1? That would be a+b≤n, a,b≥2. Their count is sum_{a=2}^{n-2} (n+1-a -1) = sum_{a=2}^{n-2} (n-a) = sum_{j=2}^{n-2} j = (n-2)(n-1)/2 - 1? Actually, let's compute properly: Points with a,b≥2, a+b≤n+1, and not on sum=n+1: those with sum ≤ n. So number = sum_{s=4}^{n} (s-3) = (n-2)(n-1)/2? Wait, for s=4 to n, count = sum_{k=4}^{n} (k-3) = sum_{j=1}^{n-3} j = (n-3)(n-2)/2. That's (n-2)(n-3)/2, which matches uncovered. So uncovered points are those with a,b≥2, a+b≤n (i.e., sum ≤ n). That's a triangle of points with a,b≥2, a+b≤n. Their number is (n-2)(n-3)/2.

Now, we have 2 sunny lines and n-5 other non-sunny lines to cover these uncovered points. The non-sunny lines could be chosen as vertical or horizontal lines or slope -1 lines. But note: we already used x=1, y=1, and x+y=n+1. Could we use other slope -1 lines? For example, x+y=t for t from 2 to n? But those are non-sunny. However, the uncovered points have sum ≤ n, so lines of sum t (t≤n) could cover them. Could we choose the remaining non-sunny lines to be some of these slope -1 lines to cover many points? But we have only n-5 such lines, and the uncovered points count is (n-2)(n-3)/2. For n large, (n-2)(n-3)/2 is about n^2/2, which is much larger than n-5. So we cannot cover all uncovered points with n-5 non-sunny lines unless each covers many points. But each non-sunny line covers at most n-1 points (if slope -1, t-1; if vertical, n+1-t; if horizontal, n+1-t). So with n-5 lines, max total points covered is (n-5)*(n-1) maybe, but we need to cover (n-2)(n-3)/2 points. For n≥6, (n-2)(n-3)/2 is about n^2/2, while (n-5)(n-1) is about n^2, so possible. However, the lines must be distinct and we can choose them to cover the uncovered set efficiently. The uncovered set is the triangle with a,b≥2, a+b≤n. Can we cover all these points with, say, n-5 vertical lines? For n=6, uncovered points: a,b≥2, a+b≤6. That's points: (2,2),(2,3),(2,4); (3,2),(3,3); (4,2) — total 6 points. n-5=1 vertical line x=2 covers (2,2),(2,3),(2,4) — 3 points, leaving (3,2),(3,3),(4,2) which need to be covered by 2 sunny lines? But we have only 2 sunny lines. Could we cover those 3 points with 2 sunny lines? Each sunny line can cover at most 2 points? For n=6, M=3, so sunny line can cover up to 3 points. But can we find a sunny line that covers 3 of these points? Points: (3,2),(3,3),(4,2). Are any three collinear? (3,2)-(3,3) vertical, not sunny; (3,2)-(4,2) horizontal; (3,3)-(4,2) slope -1; so no. So max 2 points per sunny line, total 4 points, but we need 3 points, that's fine if each covers 2 points? Actually 2 sunny lines can cover at most 4 points, but we have only 3 points, so it's possible if we can cover them with 2 sunny lines (each covering 2 or 1). However, we need to cover them with sunny lines; we can choose a sunny line through (3,2) and (4,2) horizontal? Not sunny. Through (3,2) and (3,3) vertical, not sunny. Through (3,3) and (4,2) slope -1, not sunny. So no sunny pair. So each sunny line covers at most 1 point from that set. So 2 sunny lines cover at most 2 points, leaving 1 uncovered. So fail.

For n=7, uncovered points: a,b≥2, a+b≤7. That's points: (2,2)-(2,5); (3,2)-(3,4); (4,2)-(4,3); (5,2) — count = 3+2+1+1? Actually compute: a=2,b=2..5:4 points; a=3,b=2..4:3 points; a=4,b=2..3:2 points; a=5,b=2:1 point; total 10 points. n-5=2 non-sunny lines. Could we choose them to cover many points? Again, no sunny pair among them? Check (4,2)-(4,3) vertical; (4,2)-(5,2) horizontal; (4,3)-(5,2) slope -1; so no sunny pair. So each sunny line covers at most 1 point, 2 sunny lines cover at most 2 points, leaving 1 uncovered. So fail.

For n=8, uncovered: a,b≥2, a+b≤8. Points: (2,2)-(2,6):5; (3,2)-(3,5):4; (4,2)-(4,4):3; (5,2)-(5,3):2; (6,2):1; total 5+4+3+2+1=15 points. n-5=3 non-sunny lines. Again, no sunny pair among them, so 2 sunny lines cannot cover 3 points. Could we use a mix? Maybe use a slope -1 line that covers many points? For instance, x+y=5 covers (2,3),(3,2) — but those are already covered by vertical lines? Actually, if we use vertical lines x=2,3,4, we already cover those points. Adding a slope -1 line could cover new points like (5,2),(5,3)? But (5,2) and (5,3) have a=5, not on x=2,3,4; but also (5,2) sum=7, (5,3) sum=8. So slope -1 line x+y=7 covers (5,2) and (6,1) not; (5,3) sum=8, x+y=8 covers (5,3) and (6,2) etc. Could we cover the 3 leftover points with one slope -1 line? For example, x+y=7 covers (5,2) and (6,1) but (6,1) not in uncovered? Uncovered after vertical lines? Let's compute more systematically: Uncovered set U = { (a,b) : a,b≥2, a+b≤8 }. We have 3 non-sunny lines. We can choose them to be, say, x=2, x=3, x+y=8? That covers: x=2: (2,2)-(2,6) 5 points; x=3: (3,2)-(3,5) 4 points; x+y=8: (2,6) already, (3,5) already, (4,4), (5,3), (6,2), (7,1) not, (8,0) no. So new points: (4,4), (5,3), (6,2). That adds 3 points, total covered = 5+4+3=12 points, uncovered = 15-12=3 points: (4,2),(4,3),(5,2)? Wait, we need to list all U: a=2: b=2,3,4,5,6 (5); a=3: b=2,3,4,5 (4); a=4: b=2,3,4 (3); a=5: b=2,3 (2); a=6: b=2 (1). Total 15. After taking x=2 and x=3, we have covered: a=2 all b=2..6; a=3 all b=2..5. So covered: (2,2)-(2,6); (3,2)-(3,5). That's 5+4=9 points. Then adding x+y=8 covers (2,6) already, (3,5) already, (4,4), (5,3), (6,2). So new: (4,4),(5,3),(6,2). So now covered: 9+3=12 points. Uncovered: (4,2),(4,3),(5,2). That's 3 points. Need 2 sunny lines to cover these 3 points. As before, no sunny pair. So fail.

Thus it seems that with k=2, we always end up with at least 3 uncovered points that form a set with no sunny pair (they are either collinear on a line of slope 0,∞,-1). So 2 sunny lines cannot cover them. This suggests k=2 is impossible.

But we need to prove it in general. Let's attempt a general argument.

We have n lines, exactly k sunny. We want to show k cannot be 2. More generally, we need to find all possible k.

From our constructions, k=0,1,3 are always possible. Could k be larger than 3? For n=3, k=3 works. For n=4, k=3 works; k=4? Not possible because total points 10, 4 sunny lines max coverage 4*M=4*2=8 <10, and we have 0 non-sunny lines, so insufficient. For n=5, k=3 works; k=4? We argued impossible because 4 sunny lines max 12 points, need 15 points, and with 1 non-sunny line max 5 points, total 17, but the non-sunny line can only cover a few points, and the sunny lines cannot cover the remaining 10 points with 4 lines? Actually we can try to see if 4 sunny lines could cover 10 points if each covers 2.5 on average, but they can only cover up to 3 each, so 4*3=12, but we need to cover 10 points; it's possible in principle. However, we need to check if there exists a set of 4 sunny lines that together cover the 10 points. We already attempted and failed because the uncovered set after using x=1,y=1 (2 non-sunny lines) gave 10 points that needed to be covered by 4 sunny lines, but each sunny line could cover at most 2 points (since M=3 for n=5 but y=x covers 3 points, but among those 10 points, y=x covers (3,3) only? Actually, we need to compute: For n=5, if we use x=1 and y=1 as non-sunny, uncovered points are (2,2)-(2,4); (3,2)-(3,3); (4,2); (5,1)? Wait, we need to include (5,1) is on y=1, so covered. Let's compute general: For k=4, we have 4 sunny lines and 1 non-sunny line. The non-sunny line could be x=1 covering 5 points, leaving 10 points. We need to see if there exists a set of 4 lines, each not parallel to axes or slope -1, that together contain all these 10 points. Each sunny line can contain at most M=3 points (since M= floor((n+1)/2)=3). So 4 lines can contain at most 12 points. So it's possible in principle. But we need to check if such a covering exists. Let's try to find a set of 4 sunny lines that cover all 10 points. The points: (2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(2,4); (3,1),(3,2),(3,3); (4,1),(4,2); (5,1). We need to cover them. So we have three lines covering 6 points, leaving (4,1) and (5,1)? Actually we covered: (2,1),(3,2); (2,2),(3,3); (2,3),(3,1). That's 6 points: (2,1),(2,2),(2,3); (3,1),(3,2),(3,3). Left (4,1),(4,2),(5,1). That's 3 points. Need 1 sunny line to cover these 3 points. Can we find a sunny line through (4,1),(4,2),(5,1)? Check collinearity: (4,1)-(4,2) vertical; (4,1)-(5,1) horizontal; (4,2)-(5,1) slope -1; so no. Could we choose a different set of 4 lines? Maybe we can use a line that covers 3 points. So it appears that k can be 0,1,3 always. Could k be 2 for some n? We attempted for n=3,4,5,6,7,8 and failed. For n=3, k=2 impossible; for n=4, k=2 impossible; for n=5, k=2 impossible; for n=6, k=2 impossible; for n=7, k=2 impossible; for n=8, k=2 impossible. So likely k=2 is never possible. Actually, for odd n, M = (n+1)/2. Is it possible to have n sunny lines each covering M points? Since each sunny line can cover at most M points, and total needed is n*M, we would need each sunny line to cover exactly M points, and the lines must be disjoint (since points can be covered by multiple lines, but if a point is covered by two lines, then the total union would have fewer than n*M distinct points, but we need exactly N = n*M distinct points, so each point must be covered by exactly one line. So we need a partition of the triangle into n lines each with M points. The only sunny line that can have M points is y=x (as argued, because only slope 1 gives that many integer points). For n>1, we would need at least 2 such lines, but y=x is unique. So impossible. Therefore k=n is impossible for n≥4.

Could k be any other value? For instance, k=4 for n=6? That would be 4 sunny lines and 2 non-sunny lines. Need to cover 21 points. 4 sunny lines max coverage 4*3=12, 2 non-sunny lines can cover at most 2*(n+1-t) or t-1 etc. Could we choose non-sunny lines to be x=1 and y=1? Now, is there any n for which k=2 could work? Possibly if the uncovered set after non-sunny lines is a set of points that can be covered by 2 sunny lines. For k=2, we have 2 sunny lines. The crucial part is that the points not covered by the non-sunny lines (which must be covered by sunny lines) must be such that there exist 2 sunny lines that together contain them. Since sunny lines have slopes not 0,∞,-1, they can intersect the triangle in certain patterns. The uncovered set after non-sunny lines is the complement of the union of those lines in the triangle. The non-sunny lines are limited: they can be horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. If we include x=1 and y=1, the uncovered set is the triangle with a,b≥2, a+b≤n+1. That's a right triangle of points. Now, could k be 0,1,3 always possible? We already have constructions. Need to check if k=3 is possible for all n≥3. We gave a construction: Use the n-3 vertical lines x=1,2,...,n-3 (non-sunny). Then the uncovered points are those with a = n-2, n-1, n (and b≥1). Those points are exactly U = {(n-2,1),(n-2,2),(n-2,3), (n-1,1),(n-1,2), (n,1)} — 6 points. We then take 3 sunny lines: y=x-(n-2), y = -x/2 + (n-2) +? Wait, we need to ensure the lines are sunny. For general n, we can use the three lines: L3: through (n-2,1) and (n-1,2) — slope 1, equation y = x - (n-2). That's sunny. L4: through (n-2,2) and (n,1) — slope (1-2)/(n-(n-2)) = -1/2, equation y = -1/2 x + (n-2) +? Actually, compute: line through (n-2,2) and (n,1): slope = -1/2, intercept: y = -1/2(x - (n-2)) + 2 = -1/2 x + (n-2)/2 + 2 = -1/2 x + (n+2)/2? Let's compute: (n-2)/2 + 2 = (n-2+4)/2 = (n+2)/2. So equation y = -x/2 + (n+2)/2. That's sunny (slope -1/2, not 0,∞,-1). L5: through (n-2,3) and (n-1,1) — slope (1-3)/(n-1-(n-2)) = -2/1 = -2, equation y = -2(x - (n-2)) + 3 = -2x + 2(n-2) + 3 = -2x + 2n -4 +3 = -2x + 2n -1. That's sunny (slope -2). These three lines are distinct and sunny. They cover all 6 points. So indeed k=3 works for all n≥3.

Now, what about k=0,1? Already have constructions: k=0: take lines x+y=2,3,...,n+1 (n lines, all non-sunny). k=1: take vertical lines x=1,...,n-1 (n-1 lines) and line y=x-(n-1) (sunny). That works for all n≥3? Check n=3: x=1,2 (2 vertical) and y=x-2 (sunny). Points: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3) covered by x=1; (2,1),(2,2) covered by x=2; (3,1) covered by y=x-2? At x=3, y=1, yes. (2,3) not in set? For n=3, points: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(2,1),(2,2),(3,1). So all covered. So k=1 works.

Thus we have constructions for k=0,1,3 for all n≥3. Now, are there any other possible k? For n=3, k=3 works, k=2 does not, k=0,1 works. So possible k: 0,1,3. For n=4, k=3 works, k=0,1 works, k=2 does not, k=4? Not possible as argued because n*M < N for even n? For n=4, N=10, M=2, n*M=8 <10, so k=4 impossible. So only 0,1,3. For n=5, k=3 works, k=0,1 works, k=4? 4 sunny lines max coverage 12 <15, impossible; k=5 impossible. So only 0,1,3. For n=6, k=3 works, k=0,1 works, k=4? 4 sunny lines max 12 <21? Now, for n=6, we need to consider if k=4 might be possible? Let's attempt general reasoning: For a given n, let k be the number of sunny lines. The other n-k lines are non-sunny. Let's analyze the structure.

We can think of the triangle T_n = { (a,b) : a,b ≥ 1, a+b ≤ n+1 }. We need to cover T_n with n lines. Each line is either vertical (x=c), horizontal (y=c), or slope -1 (x+y=t). The sunny lines are all other slopes.

Observation: The points on the line x=1 are n points. To cover them, we need either a vertical line x=1 (which covers all n points), or multiple lines each covering at most one point on that line. Since we have only k sunny lines and n-k non-sunny lines, but the non-sunny lines could be horizontal or slope -1, each covering at most 1 point on x=1. So the maximum number of points on x=1 that can be covered by lines other than x=1 is (k + (n-k)) = n lines each covering 1 point, total n points. But that would require all lines to cover distinct points on x=1. However, we also have to cover the other points. So it's possible in principle to cover all n points on x=1 using a combination of sunny and non-sunny lines, each covering one point, but then each line can cover at most one point on x=1. That would mean we need n lines to cover those n points, but we only have n lines total, so if we use all lines to cover points on x=1, then we have no lines left to cover other points. Therefore, we must have x=1 among the lines to cover the n points efficiently. Similarly, for y=1, we need y=1 among the lines. So any configuration must include the lines x=1 and y=1 as non-sunny lines (since they are the only families that can cover more than one point on those vertical/horizontal lines). Therefore, n-2 non-sunny lines must include x=1 and y=1, leaving n-4 other non-sunny lines. So we have at least 2 non-sunny lines (x=1 and y=1). That forces the sunny lines to be at most n-2, and we already have k = number of sunny lines. So k ≤ n-2.

Now, consider the points on the line x+y=n+1. There are n points on that line. They need to be covered. The only line that can cover more than one point on this line is a slope -1 line, specifically x+y=n+1 itself. So to cover all n points efficiently, we either include x+y=n+1 as a line, or use other lines each covering at most one point on that line. Since we have only n lines total, and we already have x=1 and y=1, which cover (1,n) and (n,1) respectively (two points on the line x+y=n+1). The other n-2 points on that line would need to be covered by the remaining n-2 lines (including possibly the line x+y=n+1 itself). If we do not include x+y=n+1, then we would need to cover those n-2 points with the n-2 lines (some may be sunny or non-sunny). Each such line can cover at most 1 point on x+y=n+1 (since they are not slope -1). So we would need at least n-2 lines to cover those n-2 points. But we have exactly n-2 lines left, so it's possible if each covers exactly one point on that line. However, we also have to cover all other points. So it's possible that we don't use x+y=n+1 as a line. But then the points on x+y=n+1 are covered by the other lines (each covering one point). But note: those lines also cover other points. So it's not impossible a priori.

But we can use a counting argument to bound k. Let's consider the set of points with a≥2,b≥2,a+b≤n+1. Their number is T = (n-1)(n-2)/2. These points are not on x=1 or y=1. The number of interior points is T. The number of non-sunny lines besides x=1,y=1 is n-4. Each such non-sunny line can cover at most some number of interior points. The maximum interior points a vertical line x=c (c≥2) can cover is (n+1-c -1) = n-c points (since b from 2 to n+1-c). For c=2, that's n-2 points; c=3, n-3; etc. So the sum of interior points covered by vertical lines x=2,...,x=m is sum_{c=2}^{m} (n-c) = (n-2)+(n-3)+...+(n-m) = (m-1)n - (2+3+...+m) = (m-1)n - (m(m+1)/2 -1) = (m-1)n - (m^2+m)/2 +1. If we choose vertical lines, we can cover many interior points. But we have at most n-4 such lines, so we can choose the best ones to maximize coverage. Similarly, horizontal lines y=c (c≥2) cover (n+1-c -1) = n-c points.
